Ghazali's Rational Challenge to Creation by Eternal Will
Ghazali challenges philosophers to disprove creation by eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rational challenge to the philosophers regarding the creation of the world by an eternal will, and why he demands a logical proof of impossibility.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges philosophers to disprove creation by an eternal will using reason alone.

In this passage, Ghazali sets aside scriptural arguments and meets the philosophers on their own rational ground. He presents a coherent account of creation by an eternal will and demands that they show what makes it impossible. This forces the philosophers to defend their position rationally, not just by appealing to accepted doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical context: Ghazali is addressing Ibn Sina and al-Farabi directly.
Pay attention to the parallel structure: Ghazali uses reason just as the philosophers do.
2
Identify the components of the belief
The passage describes a belief about the world's creation. List the key elements: eternal will, timing of existence, prior non-existence, will at the moment of creation.
These elements form a consistent narrative.
3
Understand the challenge question
Ghazali ends with two questions: 'What prevents this belief?' and 'What makes it impossible?' Understand that he is demanding a rational demonstration, not an assertion.
This is the core of the argument – the philosophers must prove impossibility.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice that Ghazali explicitly states he is using reason alone, matching the philosophers' method. This challenges them on their own terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : إذا كان ابن سينا يسلك هذا المسلك العقلى ، غير مبال بما عسى أن يكون هناك من نصوص دينية ، تفيد بظاهرها ، حدوث العالم ؛ فإن الغزالي يعول أيضاً على العقل وحده في إثبات حدوث العالم فيقول مخاطباً ابن سينا والفارابي : بم تنكرون على من يقول : إن العالم حدث بإرادة قديمة اقتضت وجوده في الوقت الذي وجد فيه، وأن يستمر العدم إلى الغاية التي استمر إليها ، وأن يبتدئ الوجود من حيث ابتدأ ، وأن الوجود قبله لم يكن مراداً ، فلم يحدث لذلك، وأنه في وقته الذى حدث فيه مراد بالإرادة القديمة ، فحدث لذلك ، فما المانع هذا الاعتقاد وما المحيل له ؟
I say: Since Ibn Sina follows this rational path, not heeding whatever religious texts there might be that in their apparent meaning indicate the creation (ḥudūth) of the world, al-Ghazālī also relies solely on reason to prove the creation of the world, saying, addressing Ibn Sīnā and al-Fārābī: What do you deny of one who says: The world originated (ḥadatha) by an eternal will (irāda qadīma) that necessitated its existence at the time it existed, and that non-existence continued up to the limit to which it continued, and that existence began where it began, and that before that existence was not intended (murād), so it did not occur, and that at the time it occurred it was intended by the eternal will, so it occurred. So what prevents this belief and what makes it impossible?
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an initial challenge. Al-Ghazālī states that since the philosophers (Ibn Sīnā and al-Fārābī) rely on reason alone and disregard religious texts that suggest the world's creation, he too will meet them on rational grounds. He then presents a coherent belief about the world's creation: the world was created by an eternal will that chose to bring it into existence at a specific moment, while non-existence persisted beforehand because the will did not intend existence earlier. Al-Ghazālī then challenges the philosophers to identify what prevents this belief and what makes it impossible. He demands a rational demonstration, not merely an appeal to accepted principles.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة (irāda qadīma – eternal will)
حدوث العالم (ḥudūth al-ʿālam – creation of the world)
مراد (murād – intended)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is arguing from revelation or scripture in this passage.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states he is using reason alone, just like the philosophers. He sets aside religious texts to engage on rational grounds.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazālī ask the philosophers to do in this passage?
A) Accept that the world is eternal.
B) Show what is impossible about the belief that the world was created by an eternal will.
C) Provide scriptural evidence for creation.
D) Deny that God has a will.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's challenge forces philosophers to provide a rational demonstration that creation by an eternal will is impossible, not just assert its impossibility from their own principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself impatient with God's timing, secretly believing that if He truly willed your good, He would have granted it sooner? How does the reality of an eternal will that chooses the perfect moment for everything challenge you to surrender your own sense of urgency?
?
When you face unanswered prayers, do you default to thinking God is unable or unwilling? How does Ghazali's rational scenario—that the eternal will can delay for a wise purpose—force you to reexamine the assumptions behind your frustration?
?
If you had to defend the belief that God created the world at a specific moment using only reason, what would you say? Are you confident in your answer, or do you rely solely on tradition? Let this question expose whether your faith is intellectually grounded or merely inherited.

Ghazali's Infinite Regress Argument from Celestial Rotations
Ghazali argues that the pre-eternity of the world leads to impossible mathematical proportions and paradoxical even/odd status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the impossibility of actual infinities in celestial rotations to argue against the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
An infinite number of past rotations contradicts proportional ratios and even/odd classification.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali presents a powerful critique of the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal. He shows that if the world had no beginning, the number of past celestial rotations would be infinite, yet these rotations have definite proportional ratios (like Saturn's period being 30 times the sun's). This leads to logical absurdities. Additionally, the infinite set of rotations cannot be classified as even or odd, yet every number must be one or the other. These contradictions demonstrate the impossibility of an eternal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the specific examples Ghazali uses (Sun, Saturn, Jupiter).
Pay attention to the phrasing asking opponents how they would answer.
2
Identify the proportional ratios problem
Ghazali points out that if the number of past rotations of the sun is infinite, then the number of Saturn's rotations (which are 1/30 of the sun's) would also be infinite, yet they maintain a ratio. This is problematic because infinite sets cannot have such proportional relationships without contradiction.
Consider why having a 'one-thirtieth' of an infinite set is logically problematic.
3
Understand the even/odd paradox
The passage then asks whether the number of rotations is even, odd, both, or neither. If it is infinite, it cannot be any of these, yet every number must be either even or odd. This is another contradiction arising from the assumption of pre-eternity.
The conclusion is that the assumption of an eternal world leads to logical impossibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لا نتجاوز الزامات هذه المسألة فنقول : بم تنكرون على خصومكم إذا قالوا : قدم العالم محال ؛ لأنه يؤدى إلى إثبات دورات للفلك لا نهاية لأعدادها ، ولا حصر لآحادها ، أن لها سدساً ، وربعاً ، ونصفاً ؛ فإن فلك الشمس مع يدور في سنة ، وفلك زحل فى ثلاثين سنة ؛ فتكون أدوار زحل ثلث عشر أدوار الشمس . وأدوار المشترى نصف سدس أدوار الشمس ؛ فإنه يدور في اثنتي عشرة سنة . كما أنه لا نهاية لأعداد دورات زحل ، لا نهاية لأعداد دورات الشمس مع أنها ثلث عشرها ، بل لا نهاية لأدوار فلك الكواكب الذي يدور في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة مرة واحدة ، كما لا نهاية للحركة المشرقية التي للشمس في اليوم والليلة مرة .
فإن قال قائل : هذا مما يعلم استحالته ضرورة . فبماذا تنفصلون عن قوله ؟ بل لو قال قائل : أعداد هذه الدورات شفع أو وتر ؟ أو شفع ووتر جميعاً ؟ أو لا شفع ولا وتر ؟ فإن قلتم : شفع ووتر جميعاً ، أو لاشفع ولا وتر ، فيعلم بطلانه
Rather, we do not go beyond the implications of this issue, so we say: What do you deny of your opponents when they say: The pre-eternity (qidam) of the world is impossible because it leads to establishing cycles (dawrāt) of the celestial spheres with no end to their numbers and no limit to their individuals, such that they have a sixth, a fourth, and a half. For the sphere of the sun rotates in one year, and the sphere of Saturn rotates in thirty years, so the rotations of Saturn are one-thirtieth of the rotations of the sun. And the rotations of Jupiter are one-twelfth of the rotations of the sun, for it rotates in twelve years. Likewise, there is no end to the numbers of Saturn's rotations, no end to the numbers of the sun's rotations while they are one-thirtieth of them; nay, there is no end to the rotations of the sphere of the stars that rotates once in thirty-six thousand years, just as there is no end to the eastern motion of the sun that occurs once every day and night.
Then if someone says: This is known by necessity to be impossible. How then do you separate yourselves from his statement? Or even if someone says: Are the numbers of these cycles even (shafʿ) or odd (watr)? Or both even and odd? Or neither even nor odd? For if you say: both even and odd, or neither even nor odd, then its falsity is known.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalām argument against the eternity of the world using the impossibility of an actual infinite. Ghazālī argues that if the world were eternal, the number of past rotations of celestial spheres would be infinite. However, these rotations have definite proportions (e.g., Saturn's period is 30 years, the sun's 1 year, so for every 30 solar rotations there is 1 Saturn rotation). If the numbers are infinite, then we have infinite sets with proportional ratios (e.g., the number of Saturn rotations is one-thirtieth of the number of solar rotations). This is problematic because infinite sets cannot have such proportions without contradiction. Additionally, Ghazālī raises the even/odd paradox: if the number of cycles is infinite, it cannot be classified as even or odd, yet every number must be either even or odd. Therefore, the assumption that the world is eternal leads to logical impossibilities, proving its impossibility. This argument is a direct application of the infinite regress argument in the context of celestial motions.

Key Terms
دورات (dawrāt – rotations/cycles)
شفع (shafʿ – even)
وتر (watr – odd)
قدم العالم (qidam al-ʿālam – pre-eternity of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These mathematical arguments are outdated and irrelevant to modern discussions.
Correction: The logical structure of the argument—showing that actual infinites lead to contradictions—remains a key tool in contemporary philosophy and mathematics when discussing the nature of infinity and the beginning of the universe.

Checkpoint
What contradictory conditions does Ghazalī derive from the assumption that the world is eternal?
A) That the number of rotations must be both infinite and have proportions.
B) That the sun and Saturn have different periods.
C) That the celestial spheres are made of matter.
D) That God must exist.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of an eternal world leads to logical impossibilities concerning infinite numbers, thus the world must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument forces the opponent to consider whether the number of rotations is even or odd. Have you ever avoided pressing your own beliefs to their logical conclusion because you feared they might lead to contradictions? What would it cost you to follow the logic wherever it leads?
?
Ghazali shows that assuming an eternal world leads to absurdities. In your own life, are there areas where you act as if something is eternal or unchanging (e.g., your habits, opinions, or relationships) when in fact they should have a beginning and end? How does this lesson call you to acknowledge the temporal nature of creation, including your own mortality?
?
This argument relies on the reality of proportions and parity. Are you willing to have your faith tested by reason in this way? Or do you prefer to keep belief and reason separate? What would you say to someone who dismisses such arguments as mere sophistry without engaging them?

Ibn Sina's Argument on the Necessity of a Cause for Renewed Will
Ibn Sina argues that a new will cannot arise in the pre-eternal without a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's key objection to the temporal origination of the world: that a renewed will in the pre-eternal requires a cause, which cannot be found, so the world must be eternal.

Core Idea
A new divine will needs a cause, but none exists in pre-eternity.

This passage from Ibn Sina's 'al-Isharat' presents a core philosophical argument against the world's temporal beginning. Ibn Sina argues that any renewed action or will in God would require a motivating cause (da'in) that arises at the time of action. Since God is unchanging and simple, no such cause can exist, implying the world's emanation must be eternal. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Notice the technical terms like 'da'in' (cause/motive) and 'irada mutajaddida' (renewed will).
The passage has some OCR artifacts, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the core premise
Ibn Sina states that a renewed will cannot occur except due to a cause (da'in) and cannot be haphazard. This is the key premise. Ask yourself: why does he think this?
3
Follow the argument about states
Ibn Sina discusses the idea of 'renewal of state' and argues that without a renewal, the state remains continuous. This is crucial to his rejection of a temporal beginning.
Consider what 'state' refers to in the context of God's will.
4
Analyze the objection and response
He addresses a possible objection: that the only motive for delay is the effect's precedent non-existence. He replies that this motive is always present and thus cannot explain a first moment of creation.
This is a key part of the argument against temporal origination.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ibn Sina concludes that the effect being contingent in itself but necessary through another does not prevent its perpetual existence. This implies that the world is eternal as an effect of God.
Contrast this with the Ash'ari view that the world is created in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خذ مثلاً دليل الفلاسفة على استحالة حدوث العالم . اقرأ هذا الدليل كما يعرضه ابن سينا ، ثم اقرأه كما يعرضه الغزالى ، وتبين – بعد ذلك ـ أى ـ العرضين أوضح وأنضر ، وأقوى وأرصن . اقرأ ابن سينا في الإشارات حيث يقول : [؟ إن واجب الوجود بذاته واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته وأحواله الأولية له . وأنه لم يتميز في العدم الصريح حال الأولى به فيها أن لا يوجد شيئاً ، أو بالأشياء أن لا توجد عنه أصلا ؛ وحال بخلافها ولا يجوز أن تسنح إرادة متجددة إلا لداع ، ولا أن تسنح جزافاً . وكذلك لا يجوز أن تسنح طبيعة ، أو غير ذلك ، بلا تجدد حال وكيف تسنح إرادة : لحال تجددت ، وحال ما يتجدد كحال ما يمهد له التجدد فيتجدد ... وإذا لم يكن تجدد ، كانت حال ما لم يتجدد شيء ، حالا واحدة مستمرة على نهج واحد وسواء جعلت التجدد لأمر تيسر ، أو لأمر زال ، مثلا ، كحسن من الفعل ، وقت ما تيسر ، أو معين ، أو غير ذلك ، مما عد ، كان عد ، أو كقبح يكون له ، أو كان قد زال ، أو عائق ، أو غير ذلك كان فزال . فإن كان الداعى إلى تعطيل واجب الوجود عن إفاضة الخير والجود ؛ هو كون المعلول مسبوق العدم لا محالة ، فهذا الداعي ضعيف قد انكشف لذوى الإنصاف ضعفه ، على أنه قائم فى كل حال ، ليس في حال أولى بإيجاب السبق من حال . وأما كون المعلول ممكن الوجود فى نفسه ، واجب الوجود بغيره فليس يناقض كونه دائم الوجود بغيره (۱) ]
Take for example the philosophers' proof of the impossibility of the world's temporal origination. Read this proof as Ibn Sina presents it, then read it as al-Ghazali presents it, and see afterwards which of the two presentations is clearer, fresher, stronger, and more solid. Read Ibn Sina in al-Isharat where he says: [?] 'The Necessary Existent in itself is Necessary Existent in all His attributes and His primary states. And that He was not distinguished in absolute non-existence by a state wherein it is more fitting that He not produce a thing, or that things not originate from Him at all; and a state otherwise. It is not permissible for a renewed will to occur except due to a cause (da'in), nor to occur haphazardly. Likewise, it is not permissible for a nature or other than that to occur without a renewal of state. And how can a will occur? For a state that has been renewed, and the state of what is renewed is like the state of what is prepared for renewal so it is renewed. And if there is no renewal, the state of what has not been renewed is a single continuous state upon one pattern. Whether you make the renewal for a matter that became possible, or for a matter that ceased, for example, like the goodness of an action at the time it became possible, or a specific [time], or other than that, whatever was counted, it was counted, or like an ugliness that belongs to it, or had ceased, or an obstacle, or other than that that was [there] then ceased. If the motive for restraining the Necessary Existent from pouring forth good and generosity is that the effect is necessarily preceded by non-existence, then this motive is weak, its weakness has been revealed to those possessed of fairness, although it is present in every state; it is not more fitting in one state to necessitate precedence than another. As for the effect being possible in itself and necessary through another, it does not contradict its being perpetually existent through another.'
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's argument against the temporal origination (huduth) of the world, as quoted by the editor from the Isharat. The editor first invites the reader to compare Ibn Sina's presentation with al-Ghazali's. Ibn Sina begins by stating that the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) is necessary in all His attributes and primary states. He argues that in absolute non-existence, there was no state in which it was more fitting for God to produce something or not. Then he asserts that a renewed will (irada mutajaddida) cannot occur without a cause (da'in) and cannot be random. Similarly, any change like a new nature requires a renewal of state. If there is no renewal, the state remains one continuous state. He then considers the objection that the only reason for delay was that the effect is preceded by non-existence; he says this motive is weak because it is always present and does not make one state more fitting than another. Finally, he concludes that the effect being contingent in itself and necessary through another does not contradict its perpetual existence through that other.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
إرادة متجددة
داع
تجدد
مسبوق العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ibn Sina denies any possibility of divine action in time; actually, he argues that a new determination requires a prior cause, which cannot exist in an unchanging God.
Correction: Ibn Sina does not deny that God acts, but he argues that any new act would require a cause, and since God has no changing states, the act must be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is required for a renewed will to arise in the pre-eternal?
A) A change in the divine essence
B) A cause (da'in)
C) Pure spontaneity
D) A temporal intermediary

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument aims to show that the world must be eternal because a renewed divine will would require a cause that cannot exist in an unchanging God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for the specific prayers you made last week? Are they still 'pending' or were they eternally answered or not? How does this lesson challenge your usual way of thinking about God responding to you?
?
Ibn Sina argues that a renewed will requires a cause outside of God, and no such cause exists in pre-eternity. Consider a moment in your life when you felt God's decision was 'new' or 'unexpected'. Does this lesson force you to reconsider whether that decision was actually part of an eternal, unchanging plan? How does that feel?
?
This lesson presents a logical proof that the world is eternal, contradicting the Islamic belief in creation. Instead of dismissing it, sit with the discomfort. Does your faith rest on understanding or on trust? Use the specific reasoning from Ibn Sina to articulate where you find yourself convinced or unconvinced.

Ghazali's Concise Reformulation of the Philosophers' Proof
Ghazali condenses the proof into one principle and explains it with a dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali condenses the philosophers' proof into a core principle and exposes its logical difficulty through a dichotomy.

Core Idea
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' proof leads to an infinite regress or requires an explanation for the preponderating factor.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali masterfully distills the philosophers' argument for a temporal universe into a single concise principle. He then unfolds its implications through a logical dichotomy, revealing an inherent problem in their reasoning. This lesson will guide you through Ghazali's analysis and the pivotal questions it raises.

Guided Path
1
Read the core principle
Start by reading Ghazali's opening statement: 'It is impossible for a temporal thing to originate from the pre-eternal without any intermediary at all.' This is the essence of the philosophers' proof.
Notice the strong language: 'impossible' and 'without any intermediary'.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Ghazali then presents a logical fork: when the world does come into existence, either a preponderating factor has been renewed or it has not. Each branch leads to a problem.
3
Analyze each branch
If no new factor, the world remains purely possible; it should not exist. If a new factor, then that factor itself is temporal and requires an explanation, leading to a regress.
The key question is: who originated that preponderating factor and why now?
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how Ghazali's reformulation exposes a difficulty in the philosophers' proof: it either fails to explain the world's emergence or pushes the problem back one step.
This is not Ghazali's own proof; he is showing an implication of their reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اقرأ الغزالى ، واعجب كيف ركز دليلهم في هذه العبارة الوجيزة الجامعة : 1 يستحيل صدور حادث من قديم بغير واسطة أصلا ] ثم راح يشرحها شروحاً مختلفة يعتبر كل واحد منها كأنه دليل قائم بنفسه ، فيقول : أولاً : [ إنا إذا فرضنا القديم ولم يصدر منه العالم مثلاً، فإنما لم يصدر لأنه لم يكن للوجود مرجح ، بل كان وجود العالم ممكناً إمكاناً صرفاً فإذا حدث بعد ذلك لم يخل : إما أن يكون قد تجدد مرجح أو لم يتجدد فإذا لم يتجدد مرجح ، بقى العالم على الإمكان الصرف ، كما كان قبل ذلك . و إن تجدد مرجح ، فمن محدث ذلك المرجح ؟ ولم حدث الآن ولم يحدث من قبل ؟ والسؤال في حدوث المرجح قائم ]
Then read al-Ghazali, and wonder how he focused their proof in this brief comprehensive phrase: '1. It is impossible for a temporal thing to originate from the pre-eternal without any intermediary at all.' Then he proceeded to explain it with various explanations, each of which he considers as an independent proof. He says: First: [If we assume the pre-eternal and that the world, for example, did not originate from Him, then it did not originate only because there was no preponderating factor (murajjih) for existence, rather the existence of the world was purely possible. Then when it does occur afterwards, it is not free from either: a preponderating factor has been renewed or not renewed. If no preponderating factor is renewed, the world remains upon pure possibility, as it was before. And if a preponderating factor is renewed, then who originated (muhdith) that preponderating factor? And why did it occur now and not occur before? And the question about the temporal origination of the preponderating factor remains.]
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's method of summarizing and explaining the philosophers' proof. He first states the core principle in a concise phrase: 'It is impossible for a temporal thing (hadith) to originate from the pre-eternal (qadim) without any intermediary (wasita) at all.' This captures the essence of the argument. Then he elaborates by presenting a logical dichotomy. Assuming the pre-eternal (God) and that the world did not originally exist, the reason for non-existence is the absence of a preponderating factor (murajjih) that would tip the balance toward existence; the world's existence was merely possible. When the world does come into existence, either a new preponderating factor has emerged or it has not. If no new factor emerged, the world would remain in pure possibility, so it should not exist. If a new factor did emerge, then that factor itself is temporal (hadith), and we must ask what caused it to arise now rather than earlier. This leads to an infinite regress or requires an explanation for the factor itself, which is the same problem. Thus al-Ghazali exposes the difficulty in the philosophers' argument.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
واسطة
مرجح
إمكان صرف
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ghazali is presenting his own proof.
Correction: He is restating the philosophers' argument to show its implications, not endorsing it.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's dichotomy, if a preponderating factor is renewed, what question arises?
A) Why did the world not exist before?
B) Who originated that preponderating factor and why now?
C) Is the preponderating factor eternal?
D) Does the preponderating factor require a cause?

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's concise reformulation reveals that the philosophers' argument either fails to explain the world's origin or leads to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that without a preponderating factor, the world would never exist. What 'preponderating factor' are you waiting for in your own life to turn to God wholeheartedly? Why have you not acted now?
?
Al-Ghazali questions: 'Why now and not before?' Ask yourself: Why are you reading this lesson now and not earlier? What changed? Was that change from God or from yourself—and what does your answer imply about your need for Him?
?
The philosophers' argument crumbles when faced with the question of the preponderating factor. In your spiritual journey, what rationalizations or 'proofs' have you relied on that actually fail when examined carefully? Will you continue to hide behind them, or will you confront the real question of your relationship with your Creator?

The Impossible Origin of the Temporal from the Eternal
Ghazali presents the philosophers' proof that the world is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the world must be eternal because temporal origination from an unchanging eternal being is impossible.

Core Idea
An unchanging eternal being cannot produce a temporal event without changing.

This passage presents the philosophers' core argument for the eternity of the world. Ghazali lays out their reasoning: if God is eternal and unchanging, then any act of creation would require a change in Him, which is impossible. Therefore, the world must have always existed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the two numbered sections: 'secondly' and 'thirdly'.
2
Understand the 'secondly' point
Identify the claim: if the Eternal's states are similar, either nothing proceeds or something exists always. Distinguishing abstention from commencement is impossible.
This sets up the principle that the Eternal cannot change its state.
3
Work through the 'thirdly' verification
Follow the chain of reasoning: why did the world not occur before? Possible reasons are examined and rejected. The only plausible reason (lack of will) leads to a new problem—will would have to change, which is impossible. This leads to infinite regress or the conclusion that temporal origination is impossible.
Pay attention to the rejection of each alternative.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Understand the absolute statement: the occurrence of an originated thing from the Eternal without change is impossible. Therefore, if the world exists, it must be eternal.
This is the foundation for the philosophers’ claim of the world's eternity.
5
Reflect on the argument's implications
Consider how this argument challenges the traditional Islamic view of creation ex nihilo. It forces a deeper examination of divine attributes like will and power.
This argument is central to the debate on the temporal origination of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً : [ و بالجملة فأحوال القديم ، إذا كانت متشابهة فإما أن لا عنه شيء قط . و إما أن يوجد على الدوام . فأما أن يتميز حال الترك عن حال الشروع ، فهو محال ] • يوجد وثالثاً : [ وتحقيقه أن يقال : لم لم يحدث العالم قبل حدوثه ؟ لا يمكن أن يحال ذلك على عجزه عن الأحداث ، ولا على استحالة الحدوث ؛ فإن ذلك يؤدى إلى أن ينقلب القديم من العجز إلى القدرة ، والعالم من الاستحالة إلى الإمكان ، وكلاهما محال · ولا يمكن أن يقال : لم يكن قبله غرض ثم تجدد غرض · ولا يمكن أن يحال على فقد آلة ، ثم على وجودها بل أقرب ما يتخيل أن يقال : لم يرد وجوده قبل ذلك ، فيلزم أن يقال : حصل وجوده لأنه صار مريداً لوجوده بعد أن لم يكن مريداً ، فتكون قد حدثت الإرادة . وحدوثه فى ذاته محال ؛ لأنه ليس محل الحوادث . وحدوثه لا في ذاته لا يجعله مريداً . ولنترك النظر في محل حدوثه ، أليس الإشكال قائماً في أصل حدوثه ؟ وأنه من أين حدث ؟ ولم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبله ؟ أحدث الآن لا من جهة الله ؟ فإن جاز حادث من غير محدث ، فليكن العالم حادثاً لا صانع له ، و إلا فأى فرق بين حادث وحادث ؟ ، و إن حدث باحداث الله، فلم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل ؟ ألعدم آلة أو قدرة ، أو غرض ، أو طبيعة ، فلما أن تبدل ذلك بالوجود حدث ؟ عاد الإشكال بعينه أو لعدم الإرادة ؟ فتفتقر الإرادة إلى إرادة ، وكذا الإرادة الأولى و يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية . • فإذن تحقق بالقول المطلق ، أن صدور الحادث من القديم ، من غير تغير أمر من القديم - في قدرة ، أو آلة ، أو وقت ، أو غرض ، أو طبع – محال وتقدير تغير حال محال ؛ لأن الكلام فى ذلك التغير الحادث كالكلام في غيره ، والكل محال ومهما كان العالم موجوداً واستحال حدوثه ، ثبت قدمه لا محالة]
Secondly: [In summary, the states of the Eternal, if they are similar, then either nothing [proceeds] from Him at all, or something exists perpetually. But that a state of abstention could be distinguished from a state of commencement is impossible.] • Thirdly: [Its verification is to say: Why did the world not occur before its occurrence? It cannot be attributed to His inability to create, nor to the impossibility of occurrence; for that would lead to the Eternal changing from inability to power, and the world from impossibility to possibility, both of which are impossible. Nor can it be said: There was no purpose before it, then a purpose arose. Nor can it be attributed to the absence of an instrument, then to its existence. Rather, the closest thing conceivable is to say: He did not will its existence before that. Then it would be necessary that it is said: Its existence occurred because He became willing of its existence after not being willing, so will would have come to be. But its coming to be in His essence is impossible, because He is not a locus of accidents. And its coming to be not in His essence does not make Him willing. Let us leave aside consideration of where it occurs. Is not the problem present in the very occurrence of the occurrence? And that from where did it occur? And why did it occur now and not before? Did it occur now not from God? For if an event is allowed without an originator, let the world be an event without a maker. What difference is there between one event and another? And if it occurred by God's causing it, why did it occur now and not before? Was it due to the absence of an instrument, or power, or purpose, or nature? Then when that changed to existence, it occurred? The same problem returns. Or due to the absence of will? Then will would require another will, and likewise the first will, leading to infinite regress. • So it is firmly established by absolute statement that the occurrence of an originated thing from the Eternal, without any change in the Eternal—in power, instrument, time, purpose, or nature—is impossible. And assuming a change of state is impossible, because the discourse concerning that originated change is the same as discourse concerning other [originated things], and all is impossible. And whenever the world exists and its temporal origination is impossible, its eternity is necessarily established.]
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof for the eternity of the world, presented by the philosophers (as quoted by Ghazali). It begins with a second point: the states of the Eternal are similar, so either nothing proceeds from Him or something exists perpetually; a change from abstention to action is impossible. The third point elaborates: why did the world not occur earlier? It cannot be due to incapacity or impossibility of occurrence because that would imply change in the Eternal. Nor can it be due to a new purpose or instrument. The only plausible answer is that He did not will it before. But then will would have to occur (originate), which is impossible because God is not a locus of occurrences, and an occurrence not in His essence does not make Him willing. The problem remains: why now? If the event occurred without a creator, any event could be uncaused. If by God's action, why now? The same problem recurs if attributed to will, leading to infinite regress. The conclusion: it is absolutely impossible for an originated thing to proceed from the Eternal without a change in the Eternal. And any change itself would be an originated thing, leading to impossibility. Hence, if the world exists and its temporal origination is impossible, its eternity is established.

Key Terms
قديم (Eternal)
حادث (originated/event)
إرادة (will)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal origination from the Eternal is possible if God simply wills it at a certain time.
Correction: The argument shows that such a new will would itself be an originated thing requiring explanation, leading to an infinite regress or an impossible change in God.

Checkpoint
According to the third point of the argument, why cannot the delay in the world's creation be attributed to God's lack of will before a certain time?
A) Because will is an accident that can occur in God's essence.
B) Because if God became willing after not being willing, that would require a change in God and the occurrence of a new will, which is impossible.
C) Because God's will is eternal and unchanging, so it is impossible for Him to will something at one time and not at another.
D) Because the world must be eternal if God's will is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that an eternal, unchanging God cannot be the source of temporal events, so the world must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is eternal and unchanging. Now consider: do you truly accept that this makes His actions unpredictable to human reason? Or do you find yourself wanting to explain 'why now' in a way that implies a change in God? How does this lesson challenge your comfort with mystery?
?
If the philosophers' argument is sound, then the world would be eternal—contradicting the Qur'anic account of creation. Where do you feel the tension in your own heart: are you willing to re-examine your assumptions about time and God's action, or do you feel a defensive need to refute this argument? What is at stake for your faith?
?
The argument reduces every human explanation for God's timing to an infinite regress. When you pray for something and it happens 'now' rather than earlier, do you ever wonder why God willed it at that moment? Can you trust His wisdom without needing to understand the 'how'? What would it take for you to surrender that need?

Ibn Sina's Proof for the Eternity of the World
Ghazali presents Ibn Sina's proof for the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's key argument for the eternity of the world and how Ghazali presents it despite disagreeing.

Core Idea
Change cannot occur in what is eternal—so the world must be eternal.

This passage shows Ghazali's intellectual fairness: he presents Ibn Sina's proof for the eternity of the world with clarity, even though he rejects it. The argument hinges on the impossibility of change in an eternal being. We'll unpack this proof and see how Ghazali sets it up.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret it.
2
Identify the core syllogism
The argument has two premises and a conclusion: (1) Change in the eternal is impossible. (2) The world exists and cannot have temporal origination. (3) Therefore, the world is eternal. Find these in the text.
Notice the phrase 'محال' (impossible) is used repeatedly.
3
Notice Ghazali's meta-commentary
After stating the proof, Ghazali asks which presentation is clearer—Ibn Sina's or his own. Reflect on why he might ask this, given that he disagrees with the conclusion.
This shows Ghazali's commitment to fair argumentation.
4
Reflect on intellectual honesty
Consider that Ghazali could present an opponent's argument clearly even without endorsing it. How does this shape your view of his method in the Tahafut?
This connects to broader themes of philosophical critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقدير تغير حال محال ؛ لأن الكلام فى ذلك التغير الحادث كالكلام في غيره ، والكل محال. ومهما كان العالم موجوداً واستحال حدوثه ، ثبت قدمه لا محالة] ذلك هو عرض ابن سينا لدليل قومه ، وهذا هو عرض الغزالى لدليل خصمه فأيهما أوضح وأدق ؟ وهل كان الغزالى يستطيع أن يعرض الدليل في صورة أدق وأوضح من هذا ، لو أنه كان يدين بما يدين به ابن سينا ؟
And considering change [in the eternal] is impossible; because the discussion about that temporal change is like the discussion about anything else, and all is impossible. And whenever the world exists and its temporal origination is impossible, its eternity is necessarily established. That is Ibn Sina's presentation of his people's proof, and this is al-Ghazali's presentation of his opponent's argument. Which of them is clearer and more precise? Could al-Ghazali have presented the argument in a more precise and clearer form than this, if he were to believe what Ibn Sina believes?
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a core argument for the eternity of the world. It begins by stating that considering change in the eternal is impossible, because the discussion about a temporal change is the same as any other temporal change, and all such changes are impossible for the eternal. This leads to the conclusion that if the world exists and its coming into existence in time is impossible, then its eternity is necessarily established. Then Ghazali comments that this is Ibn Sina's presentation of his group's proof, and then Ghazali presents his own version of the proof (as he understands it). He asks which is clearer and more precise, implying that his presentation might be clearer even though he does not believe in the eternity of the world. He wonders if he could have presented it more clearly if he were a believer in eternity. This shows Ghazali's intellectual honesty and his ability to fairly present opponents' arguments.

Ghazali's Denial of Necessary Causation
Ghazali denies necessary causation and outlines the first position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the conjunction between cause and effect is not necessary but is created by God in sequence, and you will be introduced to the first philosophical position he refutes.

Core Idea
No thing necessitates another; only God's custom links them.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the widely held philosophical assumption that causes necessarily produce their effects. He argues that what we observe is mere conjunction, not logical entailment, and that this conjunction results from God's prior decree. He then presents the first of three positions that deny his view, setting the stage for a detailed refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Read the first few lines where Ghazali denies that the conjunction between cause and effect is necessary. Notice his phrasing: 'ليس ضروريا عندنا' (not necessary according to us).
This is the core thesis.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the list of examples given, such as drinking and satiety, eating and fullness, etc. Understand that Ghazali is saying these are merely habitually conjoined, not logically connected.
The examples are from everyday life, medicine, astronomy, etc.
3
Understand the reason: God's decree
Read where Ghazali attributes the conjunction to God's prior decree and creation in sequence (التساوق). This is the positive alternative to necessary causation.
He argues it is within God's power to separate these pairs.
4
See the philosophers' challenge and the burning example
Note that philosophers deny this possibility. Ghazali focuses on burning cotton and fire as a key example. He asserts that contact without burning or burning without contact is possible in principle.
5
Identify the first position
Read the introduction of three positions and the first one: the claim that fire is the agent by nature, not by choice. Ghazali rejects this, stating that God is the true agent. The passage is cut off here.
The full refutation is not present in this chunk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضروريا عندنا ! بل كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا ولا إثبات أحدهما متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ، فليس من ضرورة وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما عدم الآخر ! مثل الزى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل، وهلم إلى كل المشاهدات المقترنات فى الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه ، بخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه، غير قابل للفوت ، بل في المقدور خلق الشبع دون الأكل، وخلق الموت دون جز الرقبة ، وإدامة الحياة مع جز الرقبة ، وهلم جرا إلى جميع المقترنات وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه وادعوا استحالته والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول ، فلنعين مثالا واحداً ، وهو الاحتراق في القطن مثلا عند ملاقاة النار ، فإنا نجوز وقوع الملاقاة بينهما دون الاحتراق ، ويجوز حدوث انقلاب القطن رماداً محترقاً دون ملاقاة النار ، وهم ينكرون جوازه . وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات : المقام الأول : أن يدعى الخصم أن فاعل الاحتراق هو النار فقط ، وهو فاعل بالطبع لا بالاختيار ، فلا يمكنه الكف عما هو في طبعه ، بعد ملاقاته لمحل قابل له وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق - بخلق السواد في القطن
The conjunction between what is habitually believed to be a cause and what is habitually believed to be an effect is not necessary according to us! Rather, for any two things, neither is that nor is that this, and the affirmation of one does not imply the affirmation of the other, nor does the negation of one imply the negation of the other. So it is not necessary that the existence of one entails the existence of the other, nor that the non-existence of one entails the non-existence of the other! Like drinking and satiety, eating and fullness, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and severing of the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, purging of the stomach and use of a laxative, and so on to all observed conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to a prior decree of God, may He be exalted, by creating them in sequence, not because it is necessary in itself, incapable of being missed. Rather, it is within (God's) power to create satiety without eating, to create death without severing the neck, to prolong life with severing of the neck, and so on for all conjunctions. The philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. The examination of these endless matters would be lengthy, so let us determine a single example: namely, the burning of cotton, for example, upon contact with fire. For we allow the occurrence of contact between them without burning, and we allow the occurrence of the transformation of cotton into burnt ash without contact with fire, while they deny its possibility. And on this issue there are three positions: The first position: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is only fire, and it is an agent by nature not by choice, so it cannot refrain from what is in its nature after contacting a receptive substrate. And this is something we deny. Rather, we say: the agent of burning—by creating blackness in the cotton...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a central argument against necessary causation. Ghazali begins by denying that the observed conjunction between what is commonly called a cause and what is commonly called an effect is necessary. He asserts that two things are distinct; affirming or denying one does not entail affirming or denying the other. He gives many everyday examples: drinking and satiety, eating and fullness, etc., to show that we only observe constant conjunction, not logical implication. He attributes this conjunction to God's prior decree, creating them in sequence (al-tasāwuq), not to any intrinsic necessity. He also denies the necessary connection in examples like contact with fire and burning, where he claims God could create burning without contact or fire without burning. The philosophers deny this possibility and claim impossibility. Then Ghazali introduces three positions (maqāmāt) on this issue. He presents the first position: the opponent claims that fire is the agent by nature, not by choice, so it cannot but burn when contacting a receptive substrate. Ghazali rejects this, stating that the agent of burning is God, who creates the blackness (burning) in the cotton. (The passage is cut off, but he is in the middle of the refutation.)

Key Terms
اقتران
ضروري
تساوق
فاعل بالطبع
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies empirical regularity or observsable causal relationships altogether.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny that things are habitually conjoined; he denies that the connection is necessary and uncaused by God. He affirms that God creates the sequence regularly.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why are things like satiety and eating constantly conjoined?
A) Because eating necessarily causes satiety by its nature
B) Because God creates them in sequence by custom
C) Because there is no relationship between them
D) Because the philosophers proved it

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that no observed conjunction between cause and effect is necessary; all are sustained by God's custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take medicine, do you trust the medicine itself or God who creates the cure through it? How would your dua change if you truly believed God could bring healing without any means?
?
Recall a time when a natural cause failed you—a doctor's treatment didn't work, or a plan fell through. Did you see it as chance, or did it remind you that God is the true agent? How can this lesson deepen your reliance on Him alone?
?
If you lived with the certainty that no necessity binds cause and effect, what habits or attachments would you need to change? Are you willing to surrender your trust in secondary causes and place it entirely in God?

Ghazali's Denial of Necessary Causation: The General Principle
Ghazali states the principle of non-necessary conjunction and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's core argument that the conjunction between cause and effect is not necessary, but rather created by God's decree.

Core Idea
Correlation is not causation; God is the true cause.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents a revolutionary critique of the prevailing view of causality. He argues that what we observe as cause and effect are merely events that God has habitually created in sequence, not a necessary connection. This lesson unpacks that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify Ghazali's central statement: the conjunction between what is called a cause and an effect is not necessary.
Notice the emphatic language: 'ليس ضروريا عندنا'.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Ghazali defines two things as distinct, then argues that affirming one does not entail affirming the other. He then provides a list of examples. Observe how he moves from principle to illustration.
3
Understand the theological basis
The reason for the constant conjunction is God's prior decree, not inherent necessity. Reflect on how this preserves divine power and volition.
This is the key to Ghazali's occasionalism.

Source Text

فقد سبق الغزالى التجريبيين إلى تقرير أن التجربة لا توصل إلى يقين ، فقرر أن لا ترابط إطلاقاً بين ما يسمى سبباً وما يسمى مسبباً ، واستطاع لأول مرة - فيما أعرف - أن يعلل دعواه تعليلا مقبولا ، حيث قال : جر الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ، ليس ضروريا عندنا ! بل كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك ولا ذاك هذا ولا إثبات أحدهما متضمناً لإثبات الآخر ، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفى الآخر ، فليس من ضرورة وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر ، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما عدم الآخر ! مثل الزى والشرب ، والشبع والأكل ، والاحتراق ولقاء النار ، والنور وطلوع الشمس ، والموت وجز الرقبة ، والشفاء وشرب الدواء ، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل، وهلم إلى كل المشاهدات المقترنات فى الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه ، بخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه، غير قابل للفوت ، بل في المقدور خلق الشبع دون الأكل، وخلق الموت دون جز الرقبة ، وإدامة الحياة مع جز الرقبة ، وهلم جرا إلى جميع المقترنات وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه وادعوا استحالته والنظر فى هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول ، فلنعين مثالا واحدا ، وهو الاحتراق في القطن مثلا عند ملاقاة النار ، فإنا نجوز وقوع الملاقاة بينهما دون الاحتراق ، ويجوز حدوث انقلاب القطن رماداً محترقاً دون ملاقاة النار ، وهم ينكرون جوازه .
Indeed al-Ghazālī preceded the empiricists in affirming that experience does not lead to certainty, so he affirmed that there is absolutely no connection between what is called a cause and what is called an effect, and he was able for the first time—as far as I know—to give an acceptable justification for his claim, where he said: [the letter 'j'?] The conjunction between what is customarily believed to be a cause and what is customarily believed to be an effect is not necessary for us! Rather, any two things, not this is that nor that is this, and not the affirmation of one entails the affirmation of the other, nor does its negation entail the negation of the other, so it is not necessary that from the existence of one follows the existence of the other, nor from the non-existence of one follows the non-existence of the other! Like drinking and satiety, satiety and eating, burning and encountering fire, light and sunrise, death and cutting the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, diarrhea and using a laxative, and so on to all observed concomitances in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to the prior decree of God the Exalted, by His creating them in sequence, not because it is necessary in itself and cannot be absent. Rather, it is within [divine] power to create satiety without eating, to create death without cutting the neck, to sustain life along with cutting the neck, and so on for all concomitants. The philosophers denied its possibility and claimed its impossibility. Deliberation on these matters beyond enumeration would be lengthy, so let us specify one example, namely burning in cotton, for instance, upon contact with fire. For we allow the occurrence of contact between them without burning, and we allow the occurrence of the cotton turning into burnt ashes without contact with fire, and they deny its permissibility.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a direct argument from al-Ghazālī against the necessary connection between cause and effect. The author first notes that Ghazālī preceded modern empiricists in asserting that experience does not yield certainty. Ghazālī then delivers his famous doctrine: the conjunction between what is conventionally called a cause and what is called an effect is not necessary. He clarifies that two distinct things are not identical; affirming one does not entail affirming the other, nor does negating one entail negating the other. Therefore, the existence or non-existence of one does not necessitate the existence or non-existence of the other. He provides multiple examples from everyday life: drinking and satiety, eating and being full, burning and fire, light and sunrise, death and decapitation, recovery and medication, diarrhea and laxatives. He generalizes to all observed regularities in various fields. The reason for their constant conjunction, he explains, is God's prior decree and His creating them in sequence, not an inherent necessity. God has the power to create the effect without the usual cause or to withhold the effect even with the cause. The philosophers deny this possibility, claiming it is impossible. To focus the argument, he picks the example of cotton burning when it meets fire: Ghazālī affirms it is possible for the contact to occur without burning, and for burning to occur without contact, while the philosophers deny this.

اقتران
سبب
مسبب
ضروري
تقدير الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies any regularity in nature.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that events occur in regular patterns but denies that the cause has any necessary power; the regularity is due to God's habit.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why do we observe constant conjunction between what we call cause and effect?
A) Because the cause inherently produces the effect.
B) Because God creates them in sequence by His decree.
C) Because experience proves a necessary connection.
D) Because the effect is part of the cause's essence.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction between cause and effect is not necessary; it is God's decree that creates the sequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a trusted cause fails you—like a medicine that doesn't heal or a plan that collapses—does your heart immediately drift towards despair or blame? This lesson says the connection is not necessary. Will you let that truth reshape your immediate emotional response starting today?
?
You habitually rely on means: you work hard to earn, eat to be full, take medicine to heal. But do you actually believe in your core that God alone is the true cause? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived by this conviction?
?
This passage declares that everything happens by God's decree, not by the power of the cause. When you pray for something, do you expect the means to deliver, or do you expect God to deliver through or even without the means? Where is your trust really placed?

The Cotton and Fire Example: Challenging Necessity
Ghazali presents the cotton and fire example to illustrate his denial of necessary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's specific argument using the example of cotton and fire, and the philosophers' opposing view.

Core Idea
God can separate fire from burning.

Having established the general principle, Ghazali now focuses on a classic example: cotton burning when it meets fire. He claims it is possible for the contact to occur without burning, or for burning to occur without contact. This lesson examines that claim and the philosophers' denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the cotton example

Ghazali uses this as a concrete illustration of his general principle.
2
Contrast with the philosophers' position
Note that the philosophers deny this possibility, claiming it is impossible. Understand why they insist on a necessary connection.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this example challenges the common-sense view of causality and highlights divine omnipotence.
This is a key point in the debate about occasionalism.

First Station: Refuting Fire as Natural Agent
Ghazālī refutes the claim that fire is the agent of burning by nature and affirms God as the true agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazālī's argument that observed regularity does not prove causal agency, and that God is the true agent of burning.

Core Idea
Observation shows burning with fire, not by fire.

In this passage, Ghazālī opens his discussion on causality by presenting the first of three stations. The opponent claims that fire is the sole agent of burning, acting by nature without choice. Ghazālī denies this and asserts the true agent is God, challenging the opponent's reliance on mere observation. This lesson examines his core argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the passage and note the opponent's assertion: fire is the agent of burning, acting by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ), and cannot refrain from burning.
2
Observe Ghazālī's counterclaim
Notice how Ghazālī denies this and instead attributes the action of burning to God, describing the specific effects created by God.
He emphasizes that fire is inanimate (jamād) and has no action.
3
Examine the critique of evidence
Ghazālī argues that observation only proves burning occurs at the time of fire, not that fire is the agent. Understand the distinction between 'upon' (ʿinda) and 'by' (bi).
4
Reflect on the occasionalist implication
Consider how this argument establishes that God is the true cause behind all apparent natural events, even if they appear regular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللكلام في هذه المسألة ثلاثة مقامات : المقام الأول : أن يدعى الخصم أن فاعل الاحتراق هو النار فقط ، وهو فاعل بالطبع لا بالاختيار ، فلا يمكنه الكف عما هو في طبعه ، بعد ملاقاته لمحل قابل له وهذا مما ننكره ، بل نقول : فاعل الاحتراق - بخلق السواد في القطن والتفرق فى أجزائه ، وجعله حراقاً أو رماداً - هو الله تعالى ، إما بواسطة الملائكة ، أو بغير واسطة ، فأما النار - وهي جماد ـ فلا فعل لها . فما الدليل على أنها الفاعل ؟ وليس لهم دليل ، إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار ، والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها ، ولا تدل على الحصول بها ، وأنه لا علة له سواها . . . إلخ ] .
And for discussion on this issue there are three stations: The first station: that the opponent claims that the agent of burning is fire alone, and it is an agent by nature, not by choice, so it cannot refrain from what is in its nature after encountering a receptive substrate—and this is something we deny. Rather, we say: the agent of burning—by creating blackness in the cotton, disintegration of its parts, and making it burnt or ashes—is God the Exalted, either through the mediation of angels or without mediation. As for fire, it is an inanimate thing, it has no action. So what proof is there that it is the agent? They have no proof except observing the occurrence of burning upon contact with fire. Observation indicates occurrence upon it, but does not indicate occurrence by it, and that there is no cause besides it ... etc.]
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by al-Ghazālī of the first of three purported stations in the causality debate. He structures the discussion into three stations (maqāmāt). The first station is the opponent's claim that fire is the sole agent of burning, acting by nature not by will, and thus cannot refrain from burning when it meets a suitable object. Ghazālī denies this. His counterclaim is that the true agent of burning—the one who creates the blackness, disintegration, and reduction to ashes—is God. This may occur through angels or directly without intermediary. Fire itself is inanimate (jamād) and has no action. Ghazālī then challenges the opponent's evidence: what proof do they have that fire is the agent? Their only evidence is observing the occurrence of burning when fire meets cotton. Observation, Ghazālī argues, only proves that burning occurs concurrently with fire (at the time of fire), not that burning occurs by fire or that fire is its sole cause. The argument is a classic formulation of the occasionalist position: regularity does not prove causal efficacy.

بالطبع
بالاختيار
مشاهدة
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazālī denies fire has any role in burning, negating the observed regularity.
Correction: Ghazālī affirms the observed regularity (burning occurs when fire meets cotton) but denies that fire is the causal agent; the true agent is God.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's claim in the first station?
A) Fire is the agent of burning by nature and cannot refrain.
B) Fire is the agent of burning by choice.
C) God creates the burning without any intermediary.
D) Burning occurs only through the mediation of angels.

Key Takeaway
Ghazālī argues that observation of burning concurrent with fire does not prove fire is the agent; God is the true agent, and regularity does not imply causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ghazālī says observation only shows 'upon', not 'by'. Where in your life have you assumed that because something happened after something else, it was caused by it, thereby forgetting the true Agent? Confess that assumption to God and ask Him to make you see His hand behind every event.

Ghazali's Denial of the Agency of Fire in Burning
Ghazali argues that observation only shows co-occurrence, not causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's key argument that observed co-occurrence does not prove causation, and why he denies that fire is the true agent of burning.

Core Idea
Observation shows burning 'at' fire, not 'by' fire.

In this passage, Ghazali directly challenges the common-sense view that fire causes burning. He insists that we only witness burning when fire meets cotton, but that does not prove fire is the agent. Instead, the true agent is God. The editor then connects Ghazali's insight to modern positivism, while noting his unique commitment to prophecy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Ghazali's own words and the editor's commentary.
Notice that the passage mixes direct quotation with editorial analysis.
2
Identify Ghazali's claim about fire
Find where Ghazali states that fire is inanimate and has no action. Understand that he denies fire's agency.
He contrasts fire with God, who acts through angels or directly.
3
Examine the evidence for the claim
Ghazali says the philosophers' only evidence is observation of burning upon contact. He then distinguishes between 'at' and 'by'—a crucial logical move.
This distinction is the heart of his argument against necessary causation.
4
Note the editor's comparison with positivism
The editor points out that Ghazali anticipated modern positivists in this empirical skepticism, but he differed by not rejecting metaphysics and by relying on prophetic revelation.
This shows Ghazali's approach is not purely empirical but grounded in Islamic theology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتفرق فى أجزائه ، وجعله حراقاً أو رماداً - هو الله تعالى ، إما بواسطة الملائكة ، أو بغير واسطة ، فأما النار - وهي جماد ـ فلا فعل لها .
فما الدليل على أنها الفاعل ؟ وليس لهم دليل ، إلا مشاهدة حصول الاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار ، والمشاهدة تدل على الحصول عندها ، ولا تدل على الحصول بها ، وأنه لا علة له سواها . . . إلخ .
إن إنكار الغزالى فاعلية النار للإحراق بناء على أن مشاهدتنا للاحتراق عند ملاقاة النار ، تدل على حصول الاحتراق عند النار لا على الحصول بها، موقف يعسر زحزحة الغزالى عنه . ومهما يكن من أمر هذا الموقف، فقد سبق الغزالى به أرباب الوضعية الحديثة.
نعم إنه خالفهم فى إنكار الميتافيزيكا ، وإن كان قد ردها إلى خبر ، فمنه - فى نظر الغزالى - يمكن تلتقى المعرفة بالمغيبات المعصوم النبي
And the dispersion in its parts, and making it burning or ashes, is God Almighty, either through the angels or without intermediary. As for fire, which is inanimate, it has no action. What is the evidence that it is the agent? They have no evidence except observing the occurrence of burning upon contact with fire, and observation indicates occurrence at it, not occurrence by it, and that there is no cause other than it. The denial by al-Ghazali of the agency of fire for burning based on the fact that our observation of burning upon contact with fire indicates the occurrence of burning at fire, not occurrence by it, is a position from which it is difficult to dislodge al-Ghazali. Whatever the case of this position, al-Ghazali preceded the modern positivists in it. Indeed, he differed from them in denying metaphysics, although he reduced it to report; from it, in al-Ghazali's view, knowledge of the unseen can be obtained—the infallible Prophet.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a direct quotation from Ghazali and editorial commentary. The quotation presents Ghazali's argument that fire has no agency in burning; the true agent is God, either directly or through angels. The evidence for this is that we only observe burning when fire contacts cotton, but observation only proves that burning occurs at the time and place of fire, not that fire itself is the cause. There is no proof that fire is the sole cause. The editor then explains that Ghazali's position is difficult to refute and that he anticipated modern positivists in this empirical skepticism, though Ghazali differed by not rejecting metaphysics entirely. The editor concludes by noting that for Ghazali, knowledge of unseen realities is obtained from the infallible Prophet through revelation.

Key Terms
فاعلية
احتراق
مشاهدة
علة
وضعية
ميتافيزيكا
معصوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies causality altogether.
Correction: Ghazali denies that observed objects are true agents while affirming that God is the agent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does observation of fire and cotton prove?
A) Fire is the cause of burning.
B) Burning occurs when fire contacts cotton, but not that fire is the cause.
C) Burning is due to the nature of fire.
D) There is no connection between fire and burning.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the notion that fire is the agent of burning by arguing that observation only shows co-occurrence, not causality; true agency belongs to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a natural event—like rain falling or food nourishing your body—do you instinctively credit the immediate cause, or do you pause to recognize that God is the true agent? What would it mean for your gratitude if you truly believed that God alone brings about the burning, the rain, and the healing?
?
If fire's burning is a co-occurrence, not a causal act, then every time you touch a flame, you are relying on God's direct will. Does this thought make you more cautious or more trusting? How would you pray differently before using fire if you knew it has no inherent power?
?
The passage ends by pointing to the infallible Prophet as the source of knowledge about the unseen. Are you willing to submit your empirical assumptions—like 'fire burns by its nature'—to prophetic teachings? What observable pattern in your life have you been interpreting as self-caused, that you need to reinterpret as God's direct act?

Causality and Created Things

The Second Category: Non-Essential Philosophical Claims
Ghazali defines the second category of claims and gives eclipse astronomy as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the second category of philosophical claims is and why disputing such claims is not necessary for affirming prophethood.

Core Idea
Some philosophical claims are harmless to religion and need not be refuted.

Imam Ghazali begins by showing that not every philosophical claim threatens religion. He introduces a category of statements that, even if accepted, do not clash with any fundamental principle of faith. The example of astronomical explanations for eclipses illustrates this point clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic passage that defines the second category and gives the two eclipse examples. Notice that Ghazali presents these as purely descriptive claims without theological consequences.
Eclipses are a natural phenomenon; their explanation does not affect belief in God or prophethood.
2
Understand why they are harmless
Ghazali says that accepting these explanations does not require denying any essential religious belief. Refuting them is not a duty.
This prevents unnecessary conflict between science and faith.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why Ghazali separates claims into categories. This is a methodological move: it protects religion from being associated with false or irrelevant debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثانى : ما لا يصدم مذهبهم فيه أصلاً من أصول الدين ، وليس من ضرورة تصديق الأنبياء والرسل - صلوات الله عليهم - منازعتهم فيه ، كقولهم : إن الكسوف القمرى عبارة عن انمحاء ضوء القمر بتوسط الأرض بينه وبين الشمس ، من حيث إنه يقتبس نوره من الشمس ، والأرض كرة ، والسماء محيط بها من الجوانب ، فإذا وقع القمر في ظل الأرض ، انقطع عنه نور الشمس ؛ وكقولهم : إن كسوف الشمس معناه وقوع جرم القمر بين الناظر وبين الشمس ، وذلك عند اجتماعهما في العقدتين على دقيقة واحدة . وهذا الفن أيضاً لسنا نخوض فى إبطاله ، إذ لا يتعلق به غرض ، ومن ظن أن المناظرة في إبطال هذا من الدين ، فقد جنى على الدين وضعف أمره ، فإن هذه الأمور تقوم عليها براهين هندسية حسابية ، لا يبقى معها ريبة ، فمن يطلع عليها ، ويتحقق أدلتها ، حتى يخبر بسببها عن وقت الكسوفين ، وقدرهما ، ومدة بقائهما إلى الانجلاء ، إذا قيل له : إن هذا على خلاف الشرع ، لم يسترب فيه وإنما يسترب فى الشرع ، وضرر الشرع ممن ينصره لا بطريقه ، أكثر ضرره ممن يطعن فيه بطريق ه؛ وهو كما قيل : عدو عاقل خير من صديق جاهل . فإن قيل : فقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « إن الشمس والقمر لآيتان من آيات الله ، لا يخسفان لموت أحد ولا لحياته ، فإذا رأيتم ذلك فافزعوا إلى ذكر الله والصلاة ) ، فكيف يلائم هذا ما قالوه ؟ ، قلنا : وليس في هذا ما يناقض ما قالوه ، إذ ليس فيه إلا نفى وقوع الكسوف لموت أحد أو لحياته والأمر بالصلاة عنده ؛ والشرع الذي يأمر بالصلاة عند الزوال والغروب والطلوع ، من أين يبعد منه أن يأمر عند الكسوف بها استحباباً ؟ فإن قيل : فقد روى أنه قال فى آخر الحديث : « ولكن الله إذا تجلى لشيء خضع له » ، فيدل على أن الكسوف خضوع بسبب التجلى ؛ قلنا هذه الزدة لم يصح نقلها ، فيجب تكذيب ناقلها ؛ وإنما المروى ما ذكرناه ، كيف ولو كان صحيحاً ، لكان تأويله أهون من مكابرة أمور قطعية ، فكم من ظواهر أولت بالأدلة العقلية التي لا تنتهى فى الوضوح إلى هذا الحد . وأعظم ما يفرح به : ناصر الشرع ، بأن هذا وأمثاله على خلاف الشرع يصرح الملاحدة ، أن فيسهل عليه طريق إبطال الشرع ، إن كان شرطه أمثال ذلك . وهذا لأن البحث في العالم كونه حادثاً أو قديماً ، ثم إذا ثبت حدوثه فسواء كان كرة ، أو بسيطاً أو مسدساً ، أم مثمناً ؛ وسواء كانت السموات وما تحتها ثلاث عشرة طبقة - كما قالوه - أو أقل أو أكثر ؛ فنسبة النظر فيه إلى البحث الإلهى ، كنسبة النظر فى طبقات البصلة وعددها وعدد حب الرمان ؛ فالمقصود كونه من فعل الله فقط كيفما كان
The second section: that which does not clash with their belief in any fundamental principle of religion, and disputing them in it is not necessary for the affirmation of prophets and messengers (peace of God upon them), such as their saying: lunar eclipse is the disappearance of the moon's light by the earth's interposition between it and the sun, since it derives its light from the sun, and the earth is a sphere, and the sky surrounds it from all sides, so when the moon falls into the earth's shadow, the sun's light is cut off from it; and their saying: solar eclipse means the moon's body falling between the observer and the sun, and that occurs when they meet at the two nodes on one precise minute. And this type we also do not delve into refuting it, for no purpose is attached to it, and whoever thinks that debating in refuting this is part of religion has committed aggression against religion and weakened its affair, for these matters are established by geometrical and arithmetical proofs concerning which no doubt remains. Whoever becomes acquainted with them and verifies their proofs, such that he can predict by means of them the time of the two eclipses, their magnitude, and their duration until clearing, if it is said to him: 'This is contrary to the Sharia,' he will not doubt it but will rather doubt the Sharia. And the harm to the Sharia from one who supports it not in its proper way is greater than the harm from one who attacks it in its own way; and it is as was said: an intelligent enemy is better than an ignorant friend. If it is said: The Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed the sun and the moon are two signs from the signs of God, they are not eclipsed for anyone's death nor for anyone's life, so if you see that, hasten to the remembrance of God and prayer.' So how does this accord with what they say? We say: There is nothing in this that contradicts what they say, for it only contains the negation of the occurrence of eclipse for anyone's death or life, and the command to pray at it; and the Sharia that commands prayer at noon, sunset and sunrise, how far is it from commanding prayer at eclipse as a recommendation? If it is said: It was narrated that he said at the end of the hadith: 'But when God manifests to something, it becomes humbled,' indicating that eclipse is due to humbling because of manifestation. We say: This addition was not authentically transmitted, so its transmitter must be declared a liar; rather what is narrated is what we mentioned. Even if it were authentic, its interpretation would be easier than obstinately rejecting decisive matters. How many apparent meanings have been interpreted by rational proofs that do not reach this level of clarity? And the greatest thing that gladdens the supporter of the Sharia is that this and its like are declared by the atheists to be contrary to the Sharia, so that it becomes easy for him to invalidate the Sharia if its condition is such matters. This is because the investigation about the world is whether it is originated or eternal; then if its origination is established, it is the same whether it is a sphere, or flat, or hexagonal, or octagonal; and whether the heavens and what is beneath them are thirteen layers as they say, or fewer or more; so the proportion of investigating that to divine investigation is like the proportion of investigating the layers of an onion and their number and the number of pomegranate seeds; the point is only that it is an act of God, however it is.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the second category of philosophical claims that do not clash with the fundamentals of religion and are not necessary for affirming prophethood. Ghazali gives the example of astronomical explanations of lunar and solar eclipses. He argues that these are established by geometrical and arithmetical proofs leaving no doubt, so trying to refute them as part of religion harms religion by making it appear false. He then addresses the hadith about the Prophet saying that the sun and moon are signs of God and are not eclipsed for anyone's death, and to pray when seeing them. Ghazali says there is no contradiction: the hadith only negates that eclipses occur for a person's death or life, and it commands prayer, which is in harmony with Sharia that commands prayers at other times. He dismisses an additional narration about God manifesting to things causing submission as inauthentic, and even if authentic, interpretation is easier than rejecting decisive proofs. He concludes that insisting such things contradict Sharia only gives atheists a way to attack religion. Then he generalizes: the important thing about the world is whether it is created or eternal; its specific shape or number of heavens is irrelevant to divine investigation, like studying the layers of an onion.

Key Terms
الكسوف
انمحاء ضوء القمر
البراهين الهندسية الحسابية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of the second category of philosophical claims according to Ghazali?
A) They are necessary for affirming prophethood
B) They clash with fundamental principles of religion
C) They do not clash with any fundamental principle of religion
D) They are all false and must be refuted

Key Takeaway
The second category of philosophical claims does not affect religion, and disputing them is unnecessary for faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt threatened or defensive when learning a scientific fact (like an eclipse explanation) that seemed to contradict something you thought Islam required you to believe? Can you name one such instance from your own experience? After this lesson, will you commit to checking whether that issue is truly essential to your faith before letting it disturb your heart?
?
Ghazali calmly presents the eclipse explanations as facts, not as threats. Is there a topic you've been arguing about with others (online or in person) that might actually belong to this second category? What would it look like for you to set that argument aside and trust that your faith does not depend on winning it? Write down one specific issue you will let go of today.

Causality and Created Things
