Refuting the Philosophers' Objection: Eternal Will and Temporal Creation
Al-Ghazali refutes the objection that eternal will cannot cause a temporal world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ghazali refutes the philosophers' objection that an eternal will cannot produce a temporal world, by distinguishing between necessary, conventional, and customary causation.

Core Idea
Eternal will can create in time because causation is not always necessary and immediate.

Al-Ghazali presents the philosophers' strongest objection against the creation of the world by an eternal will. They argue that if the will is eternal and complete, the world should have existed eternally; any delay would require a new factor, which is impossible. In response, Al-Ghazali distinguishes between three types of causation, showing that the objection only applies to necessary essential causation, not to voluntary agency. This lesson unpacks his nuanced refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The philosophers claim that if the cause is complete, the effect must follow immediately. They argue that the world's delayed creation from an eternal will is impossible because no new factor distinguishes the moment of creation from any previous moment.
2
Identify Al-Ghazali's main response
Notice that Al-Ghazali does not deny the principle of causality. Instead, he argues that the impossibility of delay applies only to necessary essential causation, not to conventional or customary causation.
This is the key move in his refutation.
3
Analyze the example from divorce law
Al-Ghazali uses the example of a man pronouncing divorce but delaying the effect until tomorrow or entering a house. This shows that in conventional causation, delay is possible when the cause is linked to a future condition.
This illustrates how a volitional agent can cause an effect at a later time.
4
Apply the distinction to eternal will
Finally, Al-Ghazali compares God's eternal will to human intention in customary causation. He argues that if the eternal will is like complete intention (not mere resolve), then any delay would require an obstacle, but no obstacle exists in God's case. Thus, the philosophers' objection fails.
Think about the analogy: human resolve (عزم) is insufficient for action, but God's will is fully efficacious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا محال بين الإحالة، لأن الحادث موجب ومسبب، وكما يستحيل حادث بغير سبب وموجب، يستحيل وجود موجب قد تم بشرائط إيجابه وأركانه وأسبابه، حتى لم يبق شيء منتظر البتة، ثم يتأخر الموجب؛ بل وجود الموجب عند تحقق الموجب بتمام شروطه، ضروري، وتأخره محال، وجود الحادث الموجب بلا موجب. فقبل وجود العالم كان المريد موجوداً، والإرادة موجودة، ونسبتها إلى المراد موجودة، ولم يتجدد مريد، ولم تتجدد إرادة، ولا تجدد للإرادة نسبة لم تكن، فإن كل ذلك تغير، فكيف تجدد المراد، وما المانع من التجدد قبل ذلك؟! وحال التجدد لم يتميز عن الحال السابق في شيء من الأشياء، وأمر من الأمور، وحال من الأحوال، ونسبة من النسب، بل الأمور كما كانت بعينها ثم لم يكن يوجد المراد، وبقيت بعينها كما كانت، فوجد المراد، ما هذا إلا غاية الإحالة. وليس استحالة هذا الجنس، فى الموجب والموجب الضروري الذاتي، بل فى العرفى والوضعى، فإن الرجل لو تلفظ بطلاق زوجته، ولم تحصل البينونة في الحال، لم يتصور أن تحصل بعده، لأنه جعل اللفظ علة للحكم بالوضع والاصطلاح، فلم يعقل تأخر المعلول إلا أن يعلق الطلاق بمجيء الغد، أو بدخول الدار فلا يقع في الحال ولكن يقع عند مجيء الغد وعند دخول الدار، فإنه جعله علة بالإضافة إلى شيء منتظر، فلما لم يكن حاضراً في الوقت - وهو الغد والدخول - توقف حصول الموجب على حضور ما ليس بحاضر، فما حصل الموجب إلا وقد تجدد أمر وهو الدخول أو حضور الغد، حتى لو أراد أن يؤخر الموجب عن اللفظ غير منوط بحصول ما ليس بحاصل، لم يعقل، مع أنه الواضع وأنه المختار في تفصيل الوضع، فإذا لم يمكننا وضع هذا بشهواتنا، ولم نعقله، فكيف نعقله فى الإيجابات الذاتية العقلية الضرورية. وأما فى العادات، فما يحصل بقصدنا لا يتأخر عن القصد مع وجود القصد إليه إلا لمانع، فإن تحقق القصد والقدرة، وارتفعت الموانع، لم يعقل تأخر المقصود، وإنما يتصور ذلك فى العزم لأن العزم غير كاف في وجود الفعل، بل العزم على الكتابة لا يوقع الكتابة، ما لم يتجدد قصد، هو انبعاث في الإنسان متجدد حال الفعل. فإن كانت الإرادة القديمة في حكم قصدنا إلى الفعل، فلا يتصور تأخر المراد إلا لمانع، ولا يتصور تقدم القصد، فلا يعقل قصد في اليوم إلى إيجاد في الغد إلا بطريق العزم، ثم لا يزاد: لا يعقل إرادة في اليوم إلى إيجاد في الغد
If it is said: This is evidently impossible, because the originated is an effect and a caused thing. And just as an originated without a cause and an effect is impossible, so it is impossible that there should exist a cause that has been completed with the conditions of its causation, its pillars, and its causes, such that nothing remains to be awaited at all, and then the effect is delayed; rather, the existence of the effect when the cause is realized with all its conditions is necessary, and its delay is impossible, as is the existence of the originated effect without a cause. For before the existence of the world, the willing agent existed, the will existed, and its relation to the object of will existed, and no willing agent was renewed, no will was renewed, and no new relation of the will came to be, for all that would be change. So how could the object of will come to be? And what prevented it from coming to be before that? The state of coming to be was not distinguished from the previous state by any thing among things, any affair among affairs, any state among states, or any relation among relations; rather, all matters were exactly as they were, and then the object of will did not exist, and they remained exactly as they were, and then the object of will existed. This is nothing but the extreme of impossibility. And the impossibility of this kind is not in necessary essential causation, but in conventional and positive causation. For if a man pronounces the divorce of his wife, and separation does not occur immediately, it is inconceivable that it could occur afterward, because he made the utterance a cause for the ruling by convention and terminology. Thus the delay of the effect is not intelligible unless he suspends divorce upon the coming of tomorrow or entering the house, so it does not occur immediately but occurs upon the coming of tomorrow and upon entering the house. For he made it a cause in relation to something awaited. Since it was not present at the time—namely, tomorrow and entering—the occurrence of the effect depended on the presence of what is not present. So the effect only occurs when a new matter has come to be, namely entering or the arrival of tomorrow. Even if he wanted to delay the effect from the utterance without linking it to something not yet obtained, it would not be intelligible, even though he is the positor and the chooser in detailing the positing. Since we cannot posit this with our desires and do not find it intelligible, how can we find it intelligible in necessary, intellectual, essential causations? As for customs, what occurs by our intention does not delay from the intention with the existence of intention towards it except due to an obstacle. If intention and ability are realized and obstacles are removed, the delay of the intended is not intelligible. This is only conceivable in resolve, because resolve is not sufficient for the occurrence of the act; rather, resolve to write does not bring about writing unless a new intention is renewed, which is a renewed impulse in the person at the time of the act. If the eternal will is in the position of our intention toward the act, then the delay of the object of will is inconceivable except due to an obstacle, and the precedence of intention is inconceivable, so an intention today for a creation tomorrow is not intelligible except by way of resolve, and then it is not added: an intention today for a creation tomorrow is not intelligible.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. Al-Ghazali presents the philosophers' strongest objection against the creation of the world by an eternal will: that if the will is eternal and complete, the world should have existed eternally; its delayed existence is impossible because no new factor distinguishes the moment of creation. The philosophers argue that for a cause complete in all its conditions, the effect must follow immediately; otherwise, it is as impossible as an effect without a cause. Al-Ghazali then gives his response. He distinguishes between three kinds of causation: necessary essential causation (where delay is impossible), conventional/positive causation (as in legal rulings like divorce), and customary causation (as in voluntary human actions). In conventional causation, delay is possible only if the cause is linked to a future condition—like suspending divorce upon tomorrow's arrival. In that case, the effect occurs when the condition is met, not immediately. Since even human legislators can conceive of such delayed effects in positive law, it is not absurd to apply the same to God's eternal will. As for customary causation, human intentions produce their effects immediately unless obstacles exist; but resolve (عزم) is not full intention, so it may not produce an act. However, God's eternal will is analogous not to resolve but to complete intention, and thus no delay is conceivable unless due to an obstacle. The passage concludes that the philosophers' objection fails because they confuse necessary causation with conventional or voluntary causation.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
موجب
علة
العرفى والوضعى
العادات
العزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Al-Ghazali denies all causality or that he believes in occasionalism without distinction.
Correction: Al-Ghazali does not deny causality; he distinguishes three types (necessary, conventional, customary) and argues that the philosophers' objection mistakenly applies the rules of necessary causation to voluntary divine agency, where delay is possible without a new factor if the will is eternal and complete.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's response in this passage, why is the philosophers' objection about the impossibility of a delayed effect from an eternal will invalid?
A) Because the will of God is not truly eternal.
B) Because the impossibility of a delayed effect applies only to necessary essential causation, not to conventional or voluntary causation.
C) Because the world is not an effect of a cause.
D) Because the philosophers admit that delay is possible in human actions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali refutes the impossibility of delayed temporal creation from an eternal will by showing the philosophers confuse necessary necessitation with conventional and voluntary causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Delaying an Effect from a Complete Cause
Ghazali argues against delayed effect from a complete cause using two analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that a complete cause cannot have a delayed effect, and how he uses analogies from human conventions and intention to support his point.

Core Idea
A complete cause cannot produce a delayed effect—this is rationally inconceivable.

Imam Ghazali takes on the philosophers' claim that God's eternal will created the world at a later time. He argues that if a cause is truly complete—with all conditions fulfilled and no obstacles—its effect must occur immediately. Using everyday examples from divorce law and human action, he shows that any delay requires an incomplete cause. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic text once to get a feel for Ghazali's style. Then read the translation. Notice how he structures his argument with two distinct analogies.
The passage is one continuous argument, so read it as a whole.
2
Analyze the first analogy: conditional divorce
Ghazali uses the example of a man saying 'you are divorced if you enter the house.' The effect (divorce) is delayed because the cause is conditional on a future event. If the cause were complete now, the effect would happen now. Why can't we delay an effect without a condition?
This shows that a complete cause must produce its effect immediately.
3
Analyze the second analogy: human intention
Compare the eternal will to human intention. If it's like our intention, it produces the effect at once (unless obstacles). If it's like our resolution (azm), it's not sufficient and needs a new impulse, leading to change in God or infinite regress. Which analogy fits the philosophers' view?
Both options lead to problems for the philosophers.
4
Identify the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that a delayed effect from a complete cause is rationally impossible. If the philosophers insist on delay, they must posit an incomplete cause (like a conditional one) or fall into absurdity. What does this imply for the doctrine of an eternal will creating in time?
This refutes the philosophers' position according to Ghazali.
5
Discuss the common misunderstanding
Some might think Ghazali denies God's power to delay His act. But he is arguing about the nature of causation and completeness. God's will is not like human will in being subject to conditions. What is Ghazali's point?
He is critiquing the philosophers' model, not limiting God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاصطلاح، فلم يعقل تأخر المعلول إلا أن يعلق الطلاق بمجيء الغد، أو بدخول الدار فلا يقع في الحال ولكن يقع عند مجيء الغد وعند دخول الدار، فإنه جعله علة بالإضافة إلى شيء منتظر، فلما لم يكن حاضراً في الوقت - وهو الغد والدخول - توقف حصول الموجب على حضور ما ليس بحاضر، فما حصل الموجب إلا وقد تجدد أمر وهو الدخول أو حضور الغد، حتى لو أراد أن يؤخر الموجب عن اللفظ غير منوط بحصول ما ليس بحاصل، لم يعقل، مع أنه الواضع وأنه المختار في تفصيل الوضع، فإذا لم يمكننا وضع هذا بشهواتنا، ولم نعقله، فكيف نعقله فى الإيجابات الذاتية العقلية الضرورية

وأما فى العادات، فما يحصل بقصدنا لا يتأخر عن القصد مع وجود القصد إليه إلا لمانع، فإن تحقق القصد والقدرة، وارتفعت الموانع، لم يعقل تأخر المقصود، وإنما يتصور ذلك فى العزم لأن العزم غير كاف في وجود الفعل، بل العزم على الكتابة لا يوقع الكتابة، ما لم يتجدد قصد، هو انبعاث في الإنسان متجدد حال الفعل

فإن كانت الإرادة القديمة في حكم قصدنا إلى الفعل، فلا يتصور تأخر المقصود إلا لمانع، ولا يتصور تقدم القصد، فلا يعقل قصد في اليوم إلى قيام في الغد إلا بطريق العزم، وإن كانت الإرادة القديمة في حكم عزمنا، فليس ذلك كافياً في وقوع المعزوم عليه، بل لابد من تجدد انبعاث قصدى عند الإيجاد وفيه قول بتغير القديم، ثم يبقى عين الإشكال في أن ذلك الانبعاث أو القصد أو الإرادة أو ما شئت فسمه، لم حدث الآن ولم يحدث قبل ذلك فإما أن يبقى حادث بلا سبب أو يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية.
And [regarding] convention, it is not rationally conceivable that the effect is delayed except if the divorce is tied to the arrival of tomorrow or to entering the house, so it does not occur immediately but occurs upon the arrival of tomorrow and upon entering the house. For he made it a cause in relation to something awaited. Since it was not present at the time—namely, tomorrow and the entry—the occurrence of the effect becomes dependent on the presence of what is not present. So the effect occurs only when something new arises, namely the entry or the arrival of tomorrow. Even if one wanted to delay the effect from the utterance without tying it to something not yet obtained, it would not be rationally conceivable, even though he is the one who lays down the convention and has the choice in detailing the convention. So if we cannot conceive this in our own voluntary conventions, how can we conceive it in necessary, self-subsistent, intellectual effects?

As for habits, what occurs by our intention does not lag behind the intention while the intention exists except due to an obstacle. If intention and ability are realized and obstacles are removed, it is not rationally conceivable that the intended is delayed. This is only conceivable in the case of resolution (al-azm), because resolution is not sufficient for the existence of the act; rather, resolution to write does not cause writing to occur unless a new intention (qasd) arises, which is a fresh impulse in the human at the moment of the act.

So if the eternal will is analogous to our intention toward an act, then it is not conceivable that the intended is delayed except due to an obstacle, nor is it conceivable that the intention is prior. Thus, it is not rationally conceivable to have an intention today for an occurrence tomorrow except by way of resolution. And if the eternal will is analogous to our resolution, then that is not sufficient for the occurrence of the intended; rather, there must be a renewal of intentional impulse at the moment of bringing about existence, and that would entail change in the eternal. Then the very problem remains: why did that impulse, intention, will—whatever you call it—occur now and not before? So either it remains an event without a cause, or it leads to an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an argument by Imam Ghazali against the philosophers' claim that the world is created by an eternal will but temporally delayed. He uses two analogies: conditional divorce (conventional causation) and human intention (habitual causation).

First, he discusses conventional causation: in conditional divorce, the effect (the woman becoming forbidden) is delayed only because it is tied to a future condition (entry or arrival). The cause is not complete in the present; it depends on something awaited. If a human tried to delay an effect without such a condition, it would be irrational. Therefore, if even humans can only conceive of delay through a conditional cause, how can one conceive of an unconditionally complete cause (God's eternal will) producing a delayed effect?

Second, he examines habitual causation: human intention produces its effect immediately unless there is an obstacle. The only case of delay is resolution (azm), which is a weak intention that requires a new impulse to become effective. If the eternal will is like human intention, then the effect should come immediately. If it is like resolution, then it requires a new act, which would change the eternal and lead to an infinite regress. Thus, the philosophers' position leads to absurdity.

Ghazali's argument is that a complete cause without any pending condition or obstacle cannot have a delayed effect. Since the philosophers claim God's will is complete and eternal yet the world is delayed, they must either posit a condition (which they deny) or admit that God's will is like human resolution, which leads to infinite regress or change in God.

Key Terms
الموجب
الإرادة القديمة
العزم
القصد
تأخر المعلول
الانبعاث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali is denying that God can delay His act, implying a limitation on divine power.
Correction: Ghazali is not limiting God; he is showing that the philosophers' conception of a complete cause without conditions leads to a logical contradiction. It is their model that fails, not God's power.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, under what condition is a delayed effect rationally conceivable in human conventions?
A) When the cause is a voluntary intention
B) When the cause is tied to a future event
C) When the cause is eternal
D) When there are no obstacles

Key Takeaway
A complete cause without any pending condition or obstacle must produce its effect immediately; therefore, the philosophers' claim of a delayed creation from an eternal, complete will is rationally inconceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever delay following through on a commitment, convincing yourself that your intention is firm and time will bring it about? How does Ghazali's distinction between resolution and intention expose the gap between your desire and your action? What one area can you stop merely resolving and start acting immediately?
?
If God's will is complete and immediate, why do you sometimes pray with a sense that God will answer 'later' or 'someday', as if His will were like a human resolution? How does this lesson challenge you to trust in the immediacy of God's decree and respond with urgent obedience?
?
Think of an area in your spiritual life where you feel 'ready' but keep waiting for the 'right moment' – like repenting or starting a good habit. What hidden condition are you adding that makes your cause incomplete? How can you remove that condition and act now?

Ghazali's Challenge: Proving Impossibility of Delayed Effect
Ghazali challenges philosophers to prove impossibility of delayed effect via logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's demand for a logical proof (burhan) from the philosophers regarding the impossibility of a delayed effect from a complete cause, and his critique of their reliance on intuition and analogy.

Core Idea
Ghazali insists that claims about causality require demonstrative proof, not mere intuition.

In this passage, Ghazali directly confronts the philosophers' core argument that a complete cause cannot have a delayed effect. He summarizes their position, then challenges them to justify it either through necessary reason or a logical syllogism. If they claim necessary knowledge, he points to widespread disagreement; if they claim discursive knowledge, they must produce a middle term. Ghazali thus sets the stage for a rigorous epistemological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's summary of the philosophers' view
The passage begins with Ghazali restating the philosophers' argument: if a cause is complete with all conditions, the effect must be immediate. Yet they claim the world was delayed by thousands of years, which they consider impossible. Read this summary carefully to understand the starting point.
Notice the phrase 'وجب الموجب بتمام شروطه' – the cause complete with its conditions.
2
Identify the core challenge
Ghazali then asks the philosophers a pivotal question: how do they know that an eternal will cannot be related to temporal events? He forces them to choose between necessary reason (badihah) and discursive reasoning (nazar). This is the key move.
He uses the language of logic: 'حد أوسط' (middle term) and 'برهان' (demonstration).
3
Analyze Ghazali's refutation of their alternatives
Ghazali argues that if they claim necessary knowledge, why do so many rational people disagree? If they claim discursive knowledge, they must produce a proof. He dismisses their analogies with human will as invalid. This shows his method of exposing unsupported claims.
The term 'استعباد' (finding unlikely) is not a proof.
4
Examine the objection and Ghazali's counter
The passage ends with the philosophers objecting that their position is known by necessary reason. Ghazali begins a reply, turning the tables: the opponents also claim necessary knowledge. This is a classic logical maneuver.
Though the passage cuts off, the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورجع حاصل الكلام إلى أن وجد الموجب بتمام شروطه ولم يبق أمر منتظر، ذلك تأخر الموجب ولم يوجد في مدة لا يرتقى الوهم إلى أولها، بل آلاف سنين ومع لا تنقص منها شيئاً، ثم انقلب الموجب بغتة من غير أمر تجدد، وشرط تحقق، محال في نفسه وهو

والجواب : أن يقال : استحالة إرادة قديمة متعلقة بأحداث شيء ، أي شيء كان ، تعرفونه بضرورة العقل أو نظره ؟ ! وعلى لغتكم في المنطق ، تعرفون الالتقاء بين هذين الحدين بحد أوسط أو من غير حد أوسط ؟ فإن ادعيتم حداً أوسط وهو الطريق النظرى ، فلابد من إظهاره ، وإن ادعيتم معرفة ذلك ضرورة، فكيف لم يشارككم في معرفته مخالفوكم ، والفرقة المعتقدة لحدوث العالم بإرادة قديمة ، لا يحصرها بلد ، ولا يحصيها عدد ، ولاشك في أنهم لا يكابرون العقول عناداً مع المعرفة ؛ فلابد من إقامة برهان على شرط المنطق يدل على استحالة ذلك ؛ إذ ليس فى جميع ما ذكرتموه إلا الاستعباد والتمثيل بعزمنا وإرادتنا ، وهو فاسد ، فلا تضاهى الإرادة القديمة القصود الحادثة ، وأما الاستعباد المجرد فلا يكفي من غير برهان

فإن قيل : نحن بضرورة العقل نعلم أنه لا يتصور موجب بتمام شروطه من غير موجب ، ومجوز ذلك مكابر لضرورة العقل قلنا : وما الفصل بينكم وبين خصومكم ، إذا قالوا لكم : إنا بالضرورة نعلم
And the summary of the discourse is that if the cause exists with all its conditions and nothing remains awaited, the delay of the effect [would be inconceivable], but it did not exist for a period the imagination cannot reach its beginning, rather thousands of years with nothing decreasing from it, and then suddenly the effect comes about without any new thing, and a condition being actualized—that is impossible in itself.

And the answer: It is said: Is the impossibility of an eternal will being related to the origination of something—any thing whatsoever—known by necessary reason or by discursive reason? And in your logical language, do you know the connection between these two terms by a middle term or without a middle term? If you claim a middle term, which is the discursive path, you must show it. If you claim that knowledge is necessary, then why do your opponents not share that knowledge with you? The group that believes in the temporal origination of the world through an eternal will cannot be confined to a country nor counted in number, and there is no doubt that they are not stubbornly opposing reason while knowing. So you must produce a proof according to the conditions of logic indicating the impossibility of that; for there is nothing in all you have mentioned except finding it unlikely (isti'bad) and analogy with our resolution and will, which is invalid. So the eternal will is not comparable to temporal intentions, and mere finding unlikely is not sufficient without proof.

If it is said: By necessary reason we know that a complete cause without an effect is inconceivable, and whoever allows that is contradicting necessary reason. We say: What distinguishes you from your opponents when they say to you: By necessary reason we know...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage contains Ghazali's response to the philosophers' argument that a complete cause cannot have a delayed effect. He first summarizes their position: if the cause has all its conditions, the effect must be immediate; yet they claim the world was created after thousands of years without any new condition, which is self-contradictory. 

Then Ghazali issues a challenge: how do the philosophers know that it is impossible for an eternal will to be related to a temporal creation? Do they know it by necessary reason (badihah) or by discursive reasoning (nazar)? If by discursive reasoning, they must produce a middle term in a logical syllogism. If by necessary reason, then why do so many rational people (the mutakallimun) disagree? The widespread disagreement shows it is not self-evident. Therefore, the philosophers must produce a proper proof (burhan) following the conditions of logic.

Ghazali criticizes their arguments as mere 'isti'bad' (finding something unlikely) and invalid analogies with human will. He insists that the eternal will is incomparable to temporal intentions, and rhetorical dismissal is insufficient.

The passage ends with an objection from the philosophers, claiming that their position is known by necessary reason. Ghazali's reply begins but is cut off; he likely argues that the opponents also claim necessary knowledge, so the philosophers have no special privilege.

ضرورة العقل
نظر

استعباد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali rejects the principle that a complete cause must have an effect.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject the principle; he demands that the philosophers prove it as a necessary truth rather than assume it without evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how should the philosophers demonstrate the impossibility of a delayed effect from a complete cause?
A) By appealing to common sense
B) By producing a logical proof (burhan) with a middle term
C) By citing consensus of all philosophers
D) By using analogies from human will

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demands that the philosophers provide a logical proof (burhan) for the impossibility of a delayed effect, rather than relying on mere intuition or analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a claim about Allah's attributes—like His eternal will—do you instinctively judge it by what feels plausible to your human reason, or do you demand the kind of logical proof Ghazali insists on? Have you ever rejected a truth because it seemed unlikely (isti'bad) without examining the evidence? What would it mean for your faith if you held every theological conviction to the same standard as Ghazali holds the philosophers?
?
Ghazali points out that the philosophers' opponents are countless and rational. Do you ever dismiss opposing views in matters of aqeedah simply because they come from a different school, without honestly investigating whether their arguments might be based on necessary reason? What would it take for you to truly listen to a theological opponent and ask them 'Why do you think this is self-evident?'
?
If you are like most people, you rely on 'common sense' for many of your beliefs about Allah. But common sense is often shaped by culture and limited experience. After reading this lesson, are you willing to re-examine one Islamic belief you hold—and test whether you have a real proof (burhan) for it, or just a comfortable analogy? What would you do if you discovered that your belief rested on isti'bad?

The Impossible Infinite: Ghazali Against Eternal Rotations
Ghazali uses infinite celestial rotations to refute the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the logical impossibility of an actual infinite to refute the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal.

Core Idea
One infinite cannot be a fraction of another infinite—that is logically impossible.

Ghazali presents a powerful logical objection against the eternity of the world. If the world has always existed, then there have been an infinite number of celestial rotations. But these rotations have mathematical relations that lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks his argument, showing why an actual infinite cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation. Let Ghazali's own argumentative style come through.
Notice that he uses two independent but complementary objections.
2
Understand the fraction problem
Identify the different planetary periods. Saturn takes 30 years per orbit, Jupiter 12, and the sphere of fixed stars 36,000 years. The number of orbits of one is a fraction of another, yet both are infinite. Ask: How can one infinite be a fraction of another?
This is the first absurdity.
3
Analyse the even/odd dilemma
Consider if the total number of rotations is even or odd. An infinite number cannot be even or odd, but every number is one or the other. This forces a contradiction.
Ghazali shows that the philosophers are forced to say it is neither even nor odd, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Connect these two absurdities back to the initial claim: the world cannot be eternal because eternal rotations lead to logical impossibilities.
This is a classic kalam argument known for its clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لا نتجاوز إلزامات هذه المسألة فنقول : بم تنكرون على خصومكم إذا قالوا : قدم العالم محال ، لأنه يؤدى إلى إثبات دورات للفلك لا نهاية لأعدادها لآحادها أن لها سدساً وربعاً ونصفاً ، فإن فلك الشمس يدور في سنة ، وفلك زحل في ثلاثين سنة ، فتكون أدوار زحل ثلث عشر أدوار الشمس، وأدوار المشترى نصف سدس أدوار الشمس ، فإنه يدور في اثنتي عشرة سنة ؛ ثم كما أنه لا نهاية لأعداد دورات زحل ، لا نهاية لأعداد دورات الشمس ، مع أنه ثلث عشره ، بل لانهاية لأدوار فلك الكواكب الذي يدور في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة مرة واحدة ، كما لا نهاية للحركة المشرقية التى للشمس في اليوم والليلة مرة . فلو قال قائل : هذا مما يعلم استحالته ضرورة ، فبماذا تنفصلون عن قوله ؟ بل لو قال قائل : أعداد هذه الدورات شفع أو وتر ؟ ! أو شفع ووتر جميعاً ؟ أو لا شفع ولا وتر ؟ فإن قلتم شفع ووتر جميعاً، أو لا شفع ولا وتر ، فيعلم بطلانه ضرورة ، وإن قلتم : شفع ، فالشفع يصير وتراً بواحد ، فكيف أعوز ما لانهاية له واحد؟ ، وإن قلتم : وتر ، فالوتر يصير بواحد شفعاً ، فكيف أعوزه ذلك الواحد الذي به يصير شفعاً فيلزمكم القول : بأنه ليس بشفع ولا وتر
But we do not go beyond the implications of this problem. So we say: How do you deny your opponents when they say: The eternity of the world is impossible because it leads to establishing rotations of the celestial sphere whose numbers are infinite, while individuals of them have a sixth, a quarter, and a half. For the sphere of the sun rotates in one year, and the sphere of Saturn in thirty years, so the rotations of Saturn are one-third of ten rotations of the sun, and the rotations of Jupiter are one-half of a sixth of the rotations of the sun, for it rotates in twelve years. Then just as there is no end to the numbers of Saturn's rotations, there is no end to the numbers of the sun's rotations, while it is one-third of ten. Indeed, there is no end to the rotations of the sphere of the fixed stars which rotates once in thirty-six thousand years, just as there is no end to the eastern motion of the sun which occurs once in a day and night. So if someone says: This is known to be impossible by necessity, then how do you separate yourselves from his statement? Indeed, if someone says: Are the numbers of these rotations even or odd? Or both even and odd? Or neither even nor odd? If you say both even and odd, or neither even nor odd, then its falsity is known by necessity. And if you say even, then even becomes odd by one, so how can something infinite be lacking one? And if you say odd, then odd becomes even by one, so how can it be lacking that one by which it becomes even? Thus you are compelled to say that it is neither even nor odd.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal. Ghazali presents an implication argument: if the world is eternal, then there have been infinite celestial rotations. Different planets have different periods, so the number of rotations of one planet is a fraction of another—yet both are infinite. This leads to an infinite being a fraction of another infinite, which by necessity is impossible. Furthermore, one can ask whether the total number of rotations is even or odd. If infinite, it cannot be even or odd, but every number is either even or odd. The philosophers are forced into contradiction. This is a classic kalām argument against actual infinity.

Key Terms
دورات الفلك
لا نهاية لأعدادها
شفع ووتر
قدم العالم
السدس والربع والنصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is about physical impossibility, like planets physically colliding or the sun burning out.
Correction: Ghazali's argument is purely logical. He is not making predictions about physical events but showing that the concept of an actual infinite leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does Ghazali derive from the philosophers' claim of eternal celestial rotations?
A) The rotations would have stopped
B) The number of rotations would be both even and odd
C) The sun would have burned out
D) The planets would collide

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that an actual infinite leads to logical absurdities, thus the world cannot be eternal.

The Even-Odd Dilemma of Infinite Past Rotations
Ghazali presents the even-odd dilemma for infinite past rotations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the even-odd dilemma to argue that an eternal past leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
An infinite series of past rotations cannot be even or odd, yet it must be — a contradiction.

Ghazali presents a powerful dialectical argument against the eternity of the world. If the world has no beginning, then there have been an infinite number of celestial rotations. These rotations have mathematical relationships, and their total number must be either even or odd. But as Ghazali shows, neither classification is possible for an actual infinite. This argument is a classic example of using logical necessity to refute a philosophical claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the opening lines where Ghazali says the eternity of the world leads to infinite rotations with fractions. Note how he uses ratios of planetary periods.
This sets the stage for the dilemma.
2
The even-odd question
Consider the question: are the number of rotations even, odd, both, or neither? Ghazali eliminates both and neither as impossible.
3
The dilemma itself
If even, then adding one makes it odd, but an infinite set cannot lack a one to add. Similarly for odd. Thus the infinite cannot be even or odd, yet it must be.
This is the core contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: what does this imply about the possibility of an eternal universe? Ghazali concludes that eternity is impossible.

بل لا نتجاوز إلزامات هذه المسألة فنقول : بم تنكرون على خصومكم إذا قالوا : قدم العالم محال ، لأنه يؤدى إلى إثبات دورات للفلك لا نهاية لأعدادها لآحادها أن لها سدساً وربعاً ونصفاً ، فإن فلك الشمس يدور في سنة ، وفلك زحل في ثلاثين سنة ، فتكون أدوار زحل ثلث عشر أدوار الشمس، وأدوار المشترى نصف سدس أدوار الشمس ، فإنه يدور في اثنتي عشرة سنة ؛ ثم كما أنه لا نهاية لأعداد دورات زحل ، لا نهاية لأعداد دورات الشمس ، مع أنه ثلث عشره ، بل لانهاية لأدوار فلك الكواكب الذي يدور في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة مرة واحدة ، كما لا نهاية للحركة المشرقية التى للشمس في اليوم والليلة مرة . فلو قال قائل : هذا مما يعلم استحالته ضرورة ، فبماذا تنفصلون عن قوله ؟ بل لو قال قائل : أعداد هذه الدورات شفع أو وتر ؟ ! أو شفع ووتر جميعاً ؟ أو لا شفع ولا وتر ؟ فإن قلتم شفع ووتر جميعاً، أو لا شفع ولا وتر ، فيعلم بطلانه ضرورة ، وإن قلتم : شفع ، فالشفع يصير وتراً بواحد ، فكيف أعوز ما لانهاية له واحد؟ ، وإن قلتم : وتر ، فالوتر يصير بواحد شفعاً ، فكيف أعوزه ذلك الواحد الذي به يصير شفعاً فيلزمكم القول : بأنه ليس بشفع ولا وتر . فإن قيل : إنما توصف بالشفع والوتر المتناهى ، وما لا نهاية له فلا قلنا : فجملة مركبة من آحاد ، لها سدس وعشر كما سبق ، ثم لا توصف بشفع ولا وتر ، يعلم بطلانه ضرورة من غير نظر ، فبماذا تنفصلون عن هذا ؟ ! فإن قيل : محل الغلط في قولكم : أنه جملة مركبة من آحاد ، فإن هذه الدورات معدومة ، أما الماضى فقد انقرض ، وأما المستقبل فلم يوجد ، والجملة إشارة إلى موجودات حاضرة ولا موجود ههنا . قلنا : العدد ينقسم إلى الشفع والوتر ، ويستحيل أن يخرج عنه ، سواء كان المعدود موجوداً باقياً ، أو فانياً ، فإذا فرضنا عدداً الأفراس ، لزمنا أن من نعتقد أنه لا يخلو من كونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، سواء قدرناها موجودة أو معدومة ، فإن انعدمت بعد الوجود لم تتغير هذه القضية ، على أنا نقول لهم : لا يستحيل على أصلكم موجودات حاضرة ، هي آحاد متغايرة بالوصف ، ولا نهاية لها ، وهى نفوس الآدميين المفارقة للأبدان بالموت ، فهى موجودات لا توصف بالشفع والوتر ، فبم تنكرون على من يقول : بطلان هذا يعرف ضرورة ، كما ادعيم بطلان تعلق الإرادة القديمة بالأحداث ضرورة ، وهذا الرأى في النفوس ، هو الذي اختاره ابن سينا ، ولعله مذهب رسطاليس .
Rather, we do not go beyond the implications of this issue and say: Why do you deny your opponents when they say: The eternity of the world is impossible because it leads to establishing rotations of the spheres whose numbers have no end, and for their units there is a sixth, a fourth, and a half. For the sphere of the sun rotates in one year, and the sphere of Saturn in thirty years, so the rotations of Saturn are a third of a tenth of the rotations of the sun, and the rotations of Jupiter are half a sixth of the rotations of the sun, for it rotates in twelve years. Then just as there is no end to the number of Saturn's rotations, there is no end to the number of the sun's rotations, although it is a third of a tenth of it; rather, there is no end to the rotations of the sphere of the fixed stars which rotates once in thirty-six thousand years, just as there is no end to the eastern motion of the sun once per day and night. If someone said: This is known to be impossible by necessity, then how do you disentangle yourselves from his statement? Rather, if someone said: The numbers of these rotations, are they even or odd? Or both even and odd? Or neither even nor odd? For if you say both even and odd, or neither even nor odd, its falsity is known by necessity. And if you say even, then even becomes odd by one, so how can what has no end lack one? And if you say odd, then odd becomes even by one, so how can it lack that one by which it becomes even? So it necessitates you to say that it is neither even nor odd. For if it is said: Only the finite is described as even and odd, and what has no end is not. We say: Then a whole composed of units, having a sixth and a tenth as before, is not described as even or odd, and its falsity is known by necessity without reflection. So how do you disentangle yourselves from this? For if it is said: The mistake lies in your statement that it is a whole composed of units; for these rotations are non-existent: the past has passed away, and the future has not come into existence, and 'whole' refers to presently existing things, and nothing exists here. We say: Number is divided into even and odd, and it is impossible for it to fall outside that, whether the counted things are existing and enduring or perished. So if we assume a number of horses, we must believe that it is not free from being even or odd, whether we consider them existing or non-existing. For if they become non-existent after existence, this judgment does not change. Moreover, we say to them: On your own principles, it is not impossible that there are presently existing things, which are units differing in description, with no end to them, and they are the human souls that separate from bodies at death. These are existing things not described as even or odd. So why do you deny someone who says: The falsity of this is known by necessity, just as you claimed the falsity of the attachment of eternal will to temporal events is known by necessity? And this opinion about souls is what Ibn Sina chose, and perhaps it is the doctrine of Aristotle.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a cluster of arguments (إلزامات) against the eternity of the world. Ghazali presents a dialectical argument: if the world is eternal, then there have been infinite past rotations of the planets. These rotations have mathematical relationships (e.g., Saturn's rotations are 1/30 of the sun's, etc.). This already implies an infinite series with fractions, which is contradictory. He then poses the even-odd dilemma: the number of rotations must be either even or odd, or both, or neither. Both and neither are obviously false. If even, adding one makes it odd, but an infinite set cannot lack a one to add. If odd, subtracting one (or adding one to become even) similarly impossible. Thus the infinite series cannot be even or odd, but it must be — a contradiction. The opponent objects that even/odd applies only to finite numbers. Ghazali responds that the infinite series is a whole composed of units with fractions, and it is known by necessity that such a whole must be even or odd. A second objection: the rotations are non-existent (past gone, future not yet), so no actual whole exists. Ghazali counters that the classification of a number as even or odd does not depend on the present existence of the counted things; even if they are vanished, the number's property remains. He then strengthens his argument with the example of human souls: on the philosophers' own principles (specifically Ibn Sina's), souls are immaterial substances that survive death and are actual infinite in number. These souls must be either even or odd, but they cannot be, exposing another contradiction. Thus the philosophers cannot arbitrarily claim impossibility for the attachment of divine will to temporal events while accepting this.

Key Terms
دورات (rotations)
شفع (even)
وتر (odd)
عدد لا نهاية له (infinite number)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, what happens if you claim the number of rotations is even?
A) It becomes odd when you add one, but an infinite set cannot lack a one to add.
B) It remains even forever.
C) It becomes both even and odd.
D) It becomes neither even nor odd.

Key Takeaway
An actual infinite series of past events cannot be classified as even or odd, yet it must be, showing the impossibility of an eternal universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument proves the universe had a beginning, meaning a Creator brought it into existence. Are you living as if that Creator has full authority over your life, or do you act as if the universe is self-sufficient? What specific change must you make today to align your actions with the reality that your existence depends entirely on Him?
?
The even-odd dilemma shows that an infinite past is impossible, so your time is finite and every rotation of the sun brings you closer to your meeting with Allah. Have you truly internalized this, or do you live as if you have endless tomorrows? What will you do this week to ensure you are prepared for that meeting?
?
Ghazali forces a logical contradiction to expose an error in belief. Is there any area of your faith or practice where you are holding onto a contradiction—justifying something that does not align with the truth of tawheed? Ask Allah to expose any inconsistency and give you the courage to abandon it immediately.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Defending the Even-Odd Dilemma: Objections and the Souls Argument
Ghazali responds to objections and presents the souls argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's responses to objections against the even-odd dilemma, including his powerful use of the philosophers' own view on human souls.

Core Idea
Even if past rotations are non-existent, the number's parity remains, and the philosophers' infinite souls face the same even-odd impossibility.

After presenting the even-odd dilemma, Ghazali anticipates objections. The opponent might claim that even/odd only applies to finite numbers, or that the past rotations are no longer existing so no actual number exists. Ghazali counters both, and then delivers a devastating blow: on the philosophers' own principles (Ibn Sina's), human souls are infinite in number and currently existing, yet they too must be even or odd — but cannot be. This exposes an inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Objection 1: Even/odd for finite only
Read Ghazali's response: the whole composed of units with fractions must still be even or odd, known by necessity.
2
Objection 2: Rotations are non-existent
The opponent says no actual whole exists because past is gone and future not yet. Ghazali replies that number parity is independent of existence.
3
The horse example
He uses the example of a number of horses to show parity persists even if the horses perish.
4
The souls argument
Ghazali points out that on the philosophers' view, human souls are actual infinite existing things. They must be even or odd, but cannot be, so the philosophers must accept absurdity or reject their own principle.
This is a powerful refutation.
5
Conclusion
Reflect on how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own doctrines against them.

Commentary
This passage is a cluster of arguments (إلزامات) against the eternity of the world. Ghazali presents a dialectical argument: if the world is eternal, then there have been infinite past rotations of the planets. These rotations have mathematical relationships (e.g., Saturn's rotations are 1/30 of the sun's, etc.). This already implies an infinite series with fractions, which is contradictory. He then poses the even-odd dilemma: the number of rotations must be either even or odd, or both, or neither. Both and neither are obviously false. If even, adding one makes it odd, but an infinite set cannot lack a one to add. If odd, subtracting one (or adding one to become even) similarly impossible. Thus the infinite series cannot be even or odd, but it must be — a contradiction. The opponent objects that even/odd applies only to finite numbers. Ghazali responds that the infinite series is a whole composed of units with fractions, and it is known by necessity that such a whole must be even or odd. A second objection: the rotations are non-existent (past gone, future not yet), so no actual whole exists. Ghazali counters that the classification of a number as even or odd does not depend on the present existence of the counted things; even if they are vanished, the number's property remains. He then strengthens his argument with the example of human souls: on the philosophers' own principles (specifically Ibn Sina's), souls are immaterial substances that survive death and are actual infinite in number. These souls must be either even or odd, but they cannot be, exposing another contradiction. Thus the philosophers cannot arbitrarily claim impossibility for the attachment of divine will to temporal events while accepting this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the parity of a number remains even or odd even after the counted things cease to exist. Does your life's 'count' – your deeds, your moments – hold a permanent value beyond this fleeting world? What would it mean for you if every action is recorded and cannot be erased, and you must face that record? Will you live today with that reality in mind?
?
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own doctrine of souls to trap them in a contradiction. Are there any cherished beliefs in your own life that, if you followed them consistently, would lead to an absurdity you haven't faced? Will you examine your own worldview for hidden contradictions, no matter how comfortable the belief?
?
The souls argument reveals that the philosophers believed in infinite, eternal souls. Do you truly believe your soul is immortal? If so, how does that truth shape your decisions about your time, your priorities, and your loves? What would change today if you acted as though your soul's existence depends on what you do right now?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Second Counter-Objection: Philosophers Cannot Avoid Specification
Ghazali argues that philosophers cannot avoid specification, using their own concept of a total system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali demonstrates that even the philosophers' own explanation of the world's system requires specification, thereby refuting their denial of divine will.

Core Idea
Ghazali catches the philosophers in their own logic: they must admit specification, so they cannot deny a Chooser.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his second counter-objection against the philosophers' emanationist cosmology. He argues that their own account of the world's specific dimensions—its size, the number of spheres, the stars' positions—presupposes a choice among alternatives. They cannot escape the need for a specifying principle, which is none other than the divine will. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read Ghazali's Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how he structures his objection and then anticipates a philosopher's response.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument continues but the core is here.
2
Identify Ghazali's main claim
Find where Ghazali states that the philosophers have not dispensed with specification. What does he mean by 'a thing from its like'?
This is the crux: if the world could have been otherwise, why is it this way?
3
Examine the philosophers' hypothetical defense
The text after 'fa-in qultum' gives a possible philosopher objection: the total system requires this particular configuration. What is their reasoning?
They appeal to the holistic system and admit differences (large vs small).
4
Analyze Ghazali's implicit refutation
Ghazali does not directly refute here, but by presenting their defense, he shows they admit that things are distinguished from their opposites. Why would that be a problem for them?
If they admit difference, they admit specification, which implies a Specifier.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument undermines the philosophers' claim that the world emanates necessarily from God without will or choice.
This is a key move in Ghazali's overall project to defend divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في الاعتراض هو أنا نقول : أنتم في مذهبكم ما استغنيتم عن تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، فإن العالم وجد عن السبب الموجب له على هيئات مخصوصه تماثل نقائضها ، فلم اختص ببعض الوجوه ؟ ! واستحالة تمييز الشيء مثله فى الفعل أو فى اللزوم بالطبع أو بالضرورة لا تختلف عن فإن قلتم : إن النظام الكلى للعالم لا يمكن إلا على الوجه الذي وجد ، وإن العالم لو كان أصغر أو أكبر مما هو الآن عليه لكان لا يتم هذا النظام ، وكذا القول في عدد الأفلاك وعدد الكواكب ، وزعمتم أن الكبير يخالف الصغير ، والكثير يفارق القليل فما يراد منه ، فليست متماثلة بل هي مختلفة ، إلا أن القوة البشرية تضعف عن درك وجوه الحكمة فى مقاديرها وتفاصيلها ، وإنما تدرك الحكمة فى بعضها ، كالحكمة فى ميل فلك البروج عن معدل النهار ، والحكمة في الأوج والفلك الخارج عن المركز ، والأكثر لا يدرك السر فيها ، ولكن يعرف اختلافها ، ولا بعد في أن يتميز الشيء عن خلافه لتعلق نظام الأمر به، وأما الأوقات
The second aspect of the objection is that we say: You, in your doctrine, have not dispensed with specification (takhṣīṣ) of a thing from its like. For the world came into existence from the necessitating cause (al-sabab al-mūjib) in specific configurations (hayʾāt makhṣūṣa) whose opposites are similar; so why was it specified with some aspects? And the impossibility of distinguishing a thing from its like in action or in essential/necessary entailment does not differ. For if you say: The total system (al-niẓām al-kullī) of the world is only possible in the way it exists, and that if the world were smaller or larger than it is now, this system would not be complete; and likewise regarding the number of spheres and the number of stars; and you claim that the large differs from the small, and the many differs from the few – so they are not identical but different. Except that human power is too weak to perceive the aspects of wisdom in their quantities and details; it perceives wisdom in some of them, like the wisdom in the inclination of the sphere of the zodiac (falak al-burūj) from the equator, and the wisdom in the apogee (al-awj) and the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-khārij ʿan al-markaz); but most do not perceive the secret in them, yet their difference is known. And it is not far that a thing be distinguished from its opposite due to the attachment of the system of the affair to it. And as for the times
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second counter-objection (al-wajh al-thānī). Ghazali argues that the philosophers themselves cannot avoid positing specification (takhṣīṣ) in their own system. They claim the world came into existence from a necessitating cause (al-sabab al-mūjib) with specific configurations whose opposites are similar – i.e., the world could have been otherwise but it is not. Why was it specified with these particular aspects? The impossibility of distinguishing a thing from its like is irrelevant here. Ghazali then presents a hypothetical defense from the philosophers (beginning with 'fa-in qultum' 'for if you say'). They might argue that the total system (al-niẓām al-kullī) of the world is only possible in the way it exists; alternative sizes or numbers would not yield this system. The large differs from the small, the many from the few, so the alternatives are not identical but different. However, human power is too weak to perceive all the wisdoms behind these quantities; we only know some, like the wisdom in the inclination of the ecliptic and the eccentric sphere. Nevertheless, their difference is known, and it is not improbable that something is distinguished from its opposite for the sake of the system's attachment to it. The passage ends mid-sentence ('wa-ammā al-awqāt' 'and as for the times'), indicating that the argument continues beyond the window.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is accepting the philosophers' principle of 'no distinction between identicals'.
Correction: Ghazali actually uses their own admission of difference to show they need a specifying principle.

Checkpoint
What is the central point of Ghazali's second counter-objection?
A) The philosophers' explanation of the world's system requires them to admit that some things are distinguished from their opposites.
B) The philosophers have proven that the world could not have been otherwise.
C) The philosophers cannot know the wisdom behind the world's quantities.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second counter-objection forces the philosophers to admit that the world's specific features require a specifying principle, which is equivalent to divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers claimed that the total system could not be otherwise. Have you ever thought that your situation could not be otherwise—that there is no alternative? How does Ghazali's argument challenge you to see God's active choice in your life's specifics?
?
If human power is too weak to perceive the wisdom behind all of God's specifications, how does that affect your trust in Him when you cannot understand why things are the way they are? Are you willing to submit to His choice even when you see no wisdom?

The Necessity of a Specifying Attribute for Preference Between Equals
Ghazali argues that preference between two equals requires a specifying attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why voluntary choice between identical alternatives necessarily implies a specifying attribute, a key principle in kalam.

Core Idea
Preference between equals proves an attribute that distinguishes.

Ghazali uses a simple thought experiment to establish a foundational principle of kalam: all voluntary action requires a specifying attribute. By considering a person choosing between two identical dates, he shows that even when all external factors are removed, choice still occurs, forcing philosophers to admit an intrinsic attribute of specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note that Ghazali begins by clarifying he is discussing human action, not divine action.
2
Identify the thought experiment
Notice the example of two equal dates and a person who desires them. Ghazali argues that the person inevitably takes one, implying a specifying attribute.
The example is chosen to strip away any obvious reason for preference.
3
Understand the dilemma
Ghazali presents two options to the philosophers: either equality is inconceivable (which is absurd) or the person remains perplexed (which contradicts experience). Recognize how each horn is refuted.
This dilemma forces the conclusion that a specifying attribute must exist.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is crucial for kalam: it establishes that even in human action, choice cannot be explained without an attribute of specification, which is even more relevant for divine action.
This principle later applies to God's will as a specifying attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا غرض فى حق الله ، وإنما المقصود المعنى دون اللفظ ، على أنه في حقنا لا نسلم أن ذلك (۱) غير متصور ، فإنا نفرض تمرتين متساويتين بين يدى المتشوف إليهما العاجز عن تناولهما جميعاً ، فإنه يأخذ إحداهما لا محالة ، بصفة شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وكل ما ذكرتموه من المخصصات من من الحسن أو القرب أو تيسر الأخذ ، فإنا نقدر على فرض انتفائه ويبقى إمكان الأخذ ، فأنتم بين أمرين : إما أن تقولوا : إنه لا يتصور التساوى بالإضافة إلى أغراضه قط ، وهو حماقة وفرضه ممكن ، وإما أن تقولوا : التساوى إذا فرض ، بقى الرجل المتشوف أبداً متحيراً ، ينظر إليهما فلا يأخذ إحداهما بمجرد الإرادة والاختيار المنفك عن الغرض ، وهو أيضاً محال يعلم بطلانه ضرورة ، فإذن لابد لكل ناظر شاهداً أو غائباً في تحقيق الفعل الاختيارى من إثبات صفة شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله
And there is no purpose in relation to God, but the intended meaning is the concept, not the wording. However, in relation to us, we do not concede that that (1) is inconceivable. For we suppose two equal dates before a person eagerly desiring them but unable to take both; he inevitably takes one of them, by virtue of an attribute whose function is to specify one thing over its like. And everything you have mentioned as specifiers, such as beauty, proximity, or ease of taking, we can suppose their absence while the possibility of taking remains. So you are between two options: either you say that equality in relation to his purposes is never conceivable – which is foolishness, and its supposition is possible – or you say that if equality is supposed, the eager man remains perpetually perplexed, looking at them without taking either one merely by will and choice detached from purpose – and this too is impossible, its falsehood known necessarily. Therefore, for every agent, whether witnessed or unseen, in the realization of voluntary action, it is necessary to affirm an attribute whose function is to specify one thing over its like.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument of a key kalam principle: that preference (tarjih) between two exactly equal alternatives requires a specifying attribute (takhsis). Ghazali begins by clarifying that he is speaking about human action, not God's, for the moment. He then presents a thought experiment: a person desires two identical dates but can only take one. The person inevitably takes one, implying that some attribute must distinguish it, even if we cannot identify it. The philosophers might object that the choice is based on some hidden advantage, but Ghazali says we can suppose that advantage absent and still the person would take one. This forces a dilemma on the philosophers: either they deny that two things can be completely equal to the agent (which is absurd because it is conceivable), or they accept that if equality is assumed, the person would remain forever undecided, never acting. But we know from experience that people do choose, so there must be a specifying attribute. Ghazali concludes that every voluntary agent, human or otherwise, must have this attribute.

Key Terms

متشوف
إرادة
اختيار
غرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is directly arguing about God's attributes without starting from human experience.
Correction: Ghazali first establishes the principle in human action, then applies it to God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, when a person has two equal options (like two identical dates), what is necessary for them to choose one?
A) The person remains forever perplexed.
B) The person chooses one without any reason.
C) There must be a specifying attribute that distinguishes one option.
D) The options cannot be truly equal.

Key Takeaway
Preference between equally viable options requires an inherent specifying attribute, a key principle for voluntary action in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision between two equally good options, do you trust that Allah has granted you a specifying attribute to make a choice, or do you panic thinking it is random? How will you consciously rely on that attribute tomorrow?

The passage says without a specifying attribute you would remain perpetually perplexed. Recall a time you were stuck in indecision—what does that reveal about your need for Allah's guidance in even the smallest choices?
?
If every voluntary action requires a specifying attribute, then every choice you make is a sign of Allah's attributes manifest in you. Are you living with the awareness that your preferences are not trivial but part of a divine pattern? What will you change today?

The Philosophers' Need for a Specifier
Ghazali presents his second objection and the philosophers' system-based response, then counters with the problem of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the philosophers cannot avoid the need for a specifier (mukhasis) when explaining the world's specific attributes and the timing of its creation.

Core Idea
Even the philosophers' system needs a specifier for why the world is this way and not another.

Ghazali continues his refutation by turning the tables: the philosophers criticize theologians for invoking a specifier, but they themselves rely on it. He points to the world's specific size, number of spheres, and the moment of creation—all of which require a reason for being as they are. In this passage, he first presents the general problem and then tackles the philosophers' attempt to avoid it by appealing to the world's system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali frames his objection and how the philosophers respond.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the philosophers' defense
Find the part where Ghazali anticipates the philosophers saying the overall system requires specific configurations. Understand their reasoning: the world could not be otherwise without breaking the system.
3
Understand Ghazali's counter on time
Focus on the argument about times being absolutely similar. Why does this undermine the philosophers' appeal to system?
All moments are alike in possibility and order.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this shows the philosophers still need a specifier. How does the time argument force them to admit takhsis?
This sets up the next lessons on motion and the pole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في الاعتراض هو أنا نقول : أنتم في مذهبكم ما استغنيتم عن تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، فإن العالم وجد عن السبب الموجب له على هيئات مخصوصه تماثل نقائضها ، فلم اختص ببعض الوجوه ؟ ! واستحالة تمييز الشيء مثله فى الفعل أو فى اللزوم بالطبع أو بالضرورة لا تختلف عن فإن قلتم : إن النظام الكلى للعالم لا يمكن إلا على الوجه الذي وجد ، وإن العالم لو كان أصغر أو أكبر مما هو الآن عليه لكان لا يتم هذا النظام ، وكذا القول في عدد الأفلاك وعدد الكواكب ، وزعمتم أن الكبير يخالف الصغير ، والكثير يفارق القليل فما يراد منه ، فليست متماثلة بل هي مختلفة ، إلا أن القوة البشرية تضعف عن درك وجوه الحكمة فى مقاديرها وتفاصيلها ، وإنما تدرك الحكمة فى بعضها ، كالحكمة فى ميل فلك البروج عن معدل النهار ، والحكمة في الأوج والفلك الخارج عن المركز ، والأكثر لا يدرك السر فيها ، ولكن يعرف اختلافها ، ولا بعد في أن يتميز الشيء عن خلافه لتعلق نظام الأمر به، وأما الأوقات متشابهة قطعاً بالنسبة إلى الإمكان وإلى النظام ، ولا يمكن أن يدعى أنه لو خلق بعد ما خلق أو قبله بلحظة لما تصور النظام، فإن تماثل الأحوال يعلم بالضرورة : فنقول : نحن وإن كنا نقدر على معارضتكم بمثله في الأحوال إذ قال قائلون : خلقه في الوقت الذى كان الأصلح الخلق فيه ، لكنا لا نقتصر على هذه المقابلة ، بل نفرض على أصلكم تخصيصاً في موضعين لا يمكن أن يقدر فيهما اختلاف ؛ أحدهما اختلاف جهة الحركة ، والآخر تعيين موضع القطب في الحركة على المنطقة ما من (۱) • ويتصور أما القطب فبيانه أن السماء كرة متحركة على قطبين كأنهما ثابتان ، وكرة السماء متشابهة الأجزاء ، فإنها بسيطة ، لا سيما الفلك الأعلى الذي هو التاسع فإنه غير مركب (1) أصلا ، وهو متحرك على قطبين شمالي وجنوبي ، فنقول : نقطتين متقابلتين من النقط التي لا نهاية (٢) لها عندهم إلا أن يكونا ، هم ، هما القطبين ، فلم تعينت نقطتا الشمال والجنوب للقطبية والثبات ؟ ! ولم لم يكن خط المنطقة مارا بالنقتطين حتى يعود القطب إلى نقتطين متقابلتين على المنطقة ؟ ! فإن كان فى مقدار كبر السماء وشكلها حكمة ، فما الذي ميز محل القطب عن غيره ، حتى تعين لكونه قطباً، دون سائر الأجزاء والنقط ، وجميع أجزاء الكرة متساوية ؟ ! وهذا لا مخرج عنه وجميع النقط متماثلة ، فإن قيل : لعل الموضع الذى عليه نقطة القطب يفارق غيره بخاصية تناسب كونه محلاً للقطب حتى يثبت ، فكأنه لا يفارق مكانه وحيزه ووضعه أو ما يفرض إطلاقه عليه من الأسامى ؛ وسائر مواضع الفلك يتبدل بالدور وضعها من الأفلاك ، والقطب ثابت الوضع ، فلعل ذلك الموضع كان أولى بأن يكون ثابت الوضع من غيره . قلنا : ففى هذا تصريح بتفاوت أجزاء الكرة الأولى في الطبيعة ، وأنها ليست متشابهة الأجزاء ؛ وهو على خلاف أصلكم ، إذ أصل ما استدللتم به على لزوم
The second point: in objection, we say: You, in your doctrine, have not dispensed with specifying one thing over its like. For the world came into being from the necessitating cause in specific configurations whose opposites are similar; so why was it specified with some aspects? And the impossibility of distinguishing a thing from its like in action or in necessary consequence by nature or necessity does not differ. If you say: The overall system of the world is not possible except in the manner it exists; and if the world were smaller or larger than it now is, this system would not be complete; similarly regarding the number of spheres and planets; and you claim that the large differs from the small, and the many differs from the few, so they are not similar but different—except that human power falls short of grasping the aspects of wisdom in their magnitudes and details, but it grasps wisdom in some of them, like the wisdom in the inclination of the ecliptic from the celestial equator, and the wisdom in the apogee and the eccentric sphere; and most do not grasp the secret in them, but know their difference; and there is no harm in a thing being distinguished from its opposite due to the attachment of the order to it. But as for times, they are absolutely similar with respect to possibility and order; and it cannot be claimed that if He created after He created or before by a moment, the order would not be conceivable—for the similarity of states is known necessarily. So we say: We, although we are able to counter you with the same regarding states, as some say He created it at the time that was most appropriate for creation, we do not restrict ourselves to this rebuttal, but we posit upon your principle a specification in two points in which difference cannot be supposed: one is the difference in the direction of motion, and the other is the determination of the position of the pole in motion on the ecliptic. [garbled] And as for the pole, its explanation is that the heaven is a sphere moving on two poles as if they are fixed; and the sphere of heaven is similar in parts, for it is simple, especially the highest sphere which is the ninth, for it is not composite at all, and it moves on two poles, north and south. So we say: two points opposite each other from the points that are infinite (2) according to them, except that they are the poles. So why were the north and south points specified for polarity and fixity? And why did the ecliptic line not pass through the two points so that the pole would return to two opposite points on the ecliptic? If there is wisdom in the magnitude and shape of the heaven, what distinguished the place of the pole from others so that it became specified as a pole, to the exclusion of the rest of the parts and points, while all parts of the sphere are equal? There is no escape from this, and all points are similar. If it is said: Perhaps the place on which the pole point lies differs from others by a property that suits it as a place for the pole so that it is fixed; thus it does not leave its place, its space, its position, or whatever names one may apply to it; while the rest of the positions of the sphere change by rotation, their position relative to the spheres, and the pole has a fixed position; so perhaps that place was more worthy to have fixed position than others. We say: In this there is a declaration of the difference of the parts of the first sphere in nature, and that they are not similar in parts; and this is contrary to your principle, for the basis on which you argued for the necessity...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' position on the impossibility of distinguishing between equals. Ghazali begins his second objection by stating that the philosophers themselves cannot avoid specifying one thing over its equal. He points to the world: it has specific configurations (size, number of spheres, etc.) that could have been different but are not; this requires a specifier. The philosophers might respond that the overall system necessitates these specific quantities because any change would ruin the system, and even if we don't grasp all the wisdom, we know they differ. But Ghazali then attacks the case of times: all moments are exactly alike in possibility and order, so there is no reason why creation occurred at one time rather than another. He then introduces two specific cases where the philosophers face the problem: the direction of motion (not elaborated) and the determination of the celestial pole. Regarding the pole, he argues that the heavenly sphere (especially the ninth sphere) is simple and all its points are identical. Yet two opposite points are designated as fixed poles. Why those points? They could be anywhere. The philosophers might suggest that the pole's location has a special property that suits it, but Ghazali counters that this would imply that the sphere's parts are not actually identical, contradicting the philosophers' own principle that the heavens are uniform. Thus they fail to avoid the need for a specifying attribute.

نظام
تماثل
أوقات

Misconception: Philosophers think they can avoid invoking a specifier by appealing to the world's overall system and the wisdom in its details.
Correction: Ghazali shows that even with the system, the timing of creation remains unexplained because all moments are similar, forcing a need for a specifier.

Checkpoint

B) All moments are similar, so the philosophers cannot explain why creation occurred at that specific time without a specifier.
C) The philosophers have a perfect explanation for the time of creation.
D) The time of creation is irrelevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway

What would it look like to acknowledge His role in the timing of your trials and blessings?

The Problem of the Celestial Pole
Ghazali uses the example of the celestial pole to force the philosophers to admit a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the determination of the celestial poles on a uniform sphere requires a specifier, which undermines the philosophers' principle of celestial uniformity.

Core Idea
If the heavens are uniform, why are two points chosen as fixed poles?

Ghazali turns to a concrete astronomical example: the celestial sphere, especially the ninth sphere, is said to be simple and all its parts similar. Yet it rotates on two fixed poles. This seems to require a specifier to choose those points. In this passage, Ghazali presses the philosophers on this point and shows that any attempt to explain it implies a difference in the parts, contradicting their own principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the sphere and the poles. Then read the translation.
The passage is somewhat technical; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify Ghazali's question
Find the question: why are the north and south points specified as poles, while all points of the sphere are similar?
3
Consider the philosophers' hypothetical answer
Ghazali anticipates a response: maybe the pole location has a special property that suits it. Understand why that answer is problematic.
4
Understand Ghazali's response
He says that positing a special property implies the sphere's parts are not uniform, contradicting the philosophers' own principle.
This is a key refutation.

Source Text
