Third Argument: Countering with a Similar Dialectical Move
Ghazali presents a dialectical counter-argument as the third refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third argument, which uses a dialectical counter to show the opponent's reasoning is not immune to a similar objection.

Core Idea
The opponent cannot consistently deny the world could be larger or smaller without accepting a parallel argument about existence before creation.

Ghazali's third argument is a dialectical counter: he shows that even if we accept the opponent's framework, we can raise an analogous objection about the world's existence before it existed, leading to the same kind of absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument passage
Read the Arabic from 'الثالث' to 'فهذا طريق المقاومة'. It contains a nested debate; follow the back-and-forth.
Track the objections and responses carefully.
2
Identify the parallel Ghazali draws
Ghazali claims that the opponent's reasoning about size is similar to a reasoning about existence: before the world existed, it was impossible, then became possible. If they accept one, they should accept the other.
This is a tu quoque or counter-argument.
3
Understand the analogy with opposites
Ghazali uses the example of a thing with an opposite to explain how the same thing can be impossible in one state and possible in another.
This shows that state-dependence does not imply a change in God's power.

The Truth: God's Power is Absolute, No Estimation Needed
Ghazali states the truth after refuting the philosophers' excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's positive assertion that the philosophers' estimation of possibilities is meaningless, and that God's power and will are sufficient without requiring extended time.

Core Idea
God is eternal and powerful; action is never impossible for Him if He wills it—no further estimation is needed.

After refuting the excuses, Ghazali states the truth: the whole notion of 'estimation of possibilities' is meaningless. The simple Islamic position is that God is eternal and powerful; He can act whenever He wills, and no additional time or spatial constraints are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read from 'والتحقيق' to the end. This is Ghazali's positive statement after the refutations.
Notice the shift from polemics to doctrine.
2
Compare with the earlier arguments
See how Ghazali moves from refutation to affirmation. He says their estimation has 'no meaning' and replaces it with the simple belief in God's eternal power.
This is the constructive conclusion.
3
Reflect on the role of imagination
Ghazali warns that only imagination adds extra notions like extended time. Understand that reason alone does not require time for God's action.
This addresses potential confusion about divine temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's power is so absolute that even your worst sin, your deepest failing, cannot frustrate His will? If you truly believed that action is never impossible for Him, would you ever despair of His mercy? What would it mean for your life right now to live with that certainty?
?
Are you someone who adds 'something else' to God's simple power with your imagination? When you think about God, do you unconsciously impose human limitations on Him, like time or effort? Examine your heart: where do you find yourself doubting that God can act instantly and perfectly?
?
The philosophers needed an 'estimation of possibilities' to explain God's action. But you have the truth: God is eternal and powerful, and nothing is impossible for Him. Have you settled your heart on this truth, or do you still rely on human reasoning to make God's power plausible to you?

Third proof of the philosophers for the world's eternity and its objection
Ghazali presents the philosophers' third proof for the world's eternity and an objection to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' third argument for the world's eternity based on possibility, and the objection that possibility does not necessitate actual pre-eternity, drawing on an analogy with spatial finitude.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not guarantee eternal existence; the world's actual finitude disproves it.

This passage presents the philosophers' third proof for the world's pre-eternity, which relies on the concept of possibility. The argument claims that since the world was always possible, it must have always existed. Ghazali then offers an objection, comparing the temporal possibility to spatial possibility: just as the world could be larger but is finite in size, so it could have been originated at any time but actually has a beginning. The response to this objection continues on the next page.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the first part of the passage presenting the philosophers' reasoning. Note how they argue from possibility to pre-eternity.
Pay attention to the conditional statements.
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the proof step by step: possibility eternal, possible must be eternal, otherwise contradiction leads to a beginning where world impossible.
3
Study the objection
Read the objection section. Understand why Ghazali says possibility at each moment does not require actual pre-eternity.
The analogy with spatial increase is key.
4
Compare to spatial analogy
Consider how the spatial analogy works: increase is always possible, but actual infinite is impossible. Similarly, the world can be originated at any time but actual infinite duration is impossible.
This helps see the flaw in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث لهم على قدم العالم - تمسكوا بأن قالوا : وجود العالم ممكن قبل وجوده ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون ممتنعاً ثم يصير ممكناً ، وهذا الإمكان لا أول له ، أى لم يزل ثابتاً ، ولم يزل العالم ممكناً وجوده ، إذ لا حال من الأحوال يمكن أن يوصف العالم فيه بأنه ممتنع الوجود ، فإذا كان الإمكان لم يزل ، فالممكن على وفق الإمكان أيضاً لم يزل ، فإن معنى قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده ، أنه ليس محالاً وجوده ، فإذا كان ممكناً وجوده أبداً ، لم يكن محالا وجوده أبداً ، وإلا فإن كان محالا وجوده أبداً ، بطل قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده أبداً ، وإن بطل قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده أبداً ، بطل قولنا إن الإمكان لم يزل ، و إن بطل قولنا إن الإمكان لم يزل ، صح قولنا إن الإمكان له أن له أولاً ، كان قبل ذلك غير ممكن ، فيؤدى إلى إثبات أول ، فإذا صح حال لم يكن العالم ممكناً ، ولا كان الله عليه قادراً . الاعتراض : أن يقال : العالم لم يزل ممكن الحدوث ، فلا جرم ما من وقت إلا و يتصور إحداثه فيه ، وإذا قدر موجوداً أبداً لم يكن حادثاً ، فلم يكن الواقع على وفق الإمكان بل على خلافه ، وهذا كقولهم في المكان ، وهو أن تقدير العالم أكبر مما هو ، أو خلق جسم فوق العالم ، ممكن ، وكذا آخر فوق ذلك الآخر ، وهكذا إلى غير نهاية ، فلا نهاية لإمكان الزيادة ، ومع ذلك فوجود ملاء مطلق لا نهاية له ، غير ممكن ، فكذلك وجود لا ينتهى طرفه غير ممكن ، بل كما يقال : الممكن جسم متناهى السطح متناهى السطح ، ولكن لا تتعين مقاديره في الكبر والصغر ، فكذلك الممكن الحدوث ، ومبادئ الوجود لا تتعين في التقدم والتأخر ، وأصل كونه حادثاً متعين ، فإنه الممكن لا غير
A third proof of theirs for the pre-eternity of the world. They maintained by saying: The existence of the world was possible before its existence, since it is impossible that it be impossible then become possible. And this possibility has no beginning, i.e., it has always been established, and the world has always been possible in its existence, since there is no state in which the world can be described as impossible of existence. So if possibility has always been, then the possible in accordance with possibility also has always been. For the meaning of our saying that its existence is possible is that its existence is not impossible. So if its existence is always possible, then its existence is never impossible. Otherwise, if its existence were always impossible, then our saying that its existence is always possible would be false; and if our saying that its existence is always possible is false, then our saying that possibility has always been is false; and if our saying that possibility has always been is false, then our saying that possibility has a beginning (i.e., it was not possible before) becomes true, leading to the affirmation of a beginning. Then if it is established that there was a state in which the world was not possible, then God was not able over it. The objection: It may be said: The world has always been possible of origination, so inevitably there is no time except that its bringing into existence is conceivable in it. And if it is supposed to exist forever, it would not be originated. So what actually occurs is not in accordance with possibility but contrary to it. And this is like their saying about space: that the estimation of the world being larger than it is, or creating a body above the world, is possible; and similarly another above that, and so on ad infinitum. So there is no end to the possibility of increase, yet the existence of an absolute plenum with no end is not possible. So likewise an existence whose side does not end is not possible. Rather, as it is said: the possible is a body with a finite surface, but its dimensions in size and smallness are not determined. So also the possible of origination: the principles of its existence are not determined in priority and posteriority, while the fact that it is originated is determined. For it is the possible, nothing else.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' third proof for the pre-eternity of the world and then an objection. The proof runs as follows: The world's existence before it came to be was possible, and it cannot become possible after being impossible. This possibility has no beginning; the world was always possible. If possibility is eternal, then the possible must also be eternal, because if the possible were not eternal, then at some point it would be impossible, contradicting eternal possibility. This would imply a beginning before which the world was impossible, meaning God was not able to create it. The objection counters: The world has always been possible of origination at any given time, but if it existed forever, it would not be originated, so the actual state does not match its possibility. The objection draws an analogy with space: the world could be larger than it is, and one can conceive of an infinite series of larger bodies, but an actually infinite plenum is impossible. Similarly, the world's time of origin is not determined, but its being originated is essential. The actual world is finite in both spatial extent and temporal duration, even though various sizes and times are possible.

إمكان
قدم العالم
حدوث
ممكن
ملاء مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is always possible, it must actually exist forever.
Correction: Possibility at each moment does not entail actuality at all times. The world's possibility of origination is eternal, but its actual existence is finite, just as spatial increase is always possible but actual space is finite.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objection use to refute the philosophers' proof?
A) The analogy of a moving body.
B) The analogy of spatial size and the impossibility of an infinite plenum.
C) The analogy of a causal chain.
D) The analogy of divine will.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof fails because eternal possibility does not necessitate eternal existence; the world's actual finitude disproves the inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you assume that because you can always do it, it will always be there? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and call you to act now?
?
The philosophers argued that if possibility is eternal, the possible must be eternal. You might believe that God's power is eternal, but does that mean all possibilities are actualized? Reflect on a time you assumed that God must do something because He can—perhaps in relation to your own desires or judgments. How does this lesson correct that assumption and teach you to submit to God's actual will rather than your imagination of possibilities?
?
The objection uses an analogy with space: just because you can imagine larger doesn't mean an infinite plenum exists. Think about your aspirations—do you confuse what is possible with what will be? Are there dreams you hold onto as if their possibility guarantees their actuality? How can you distinguish between possibilities that are real opportunities from God and those that are merely conceivable? What would it mean to trust God's actual decree over endless possibilities?

The Third Argument for Eternity and Al-Ghazali's Objection
Third argument for world's eternity and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali refutes the philosophers' third argument for the eternity of the world by distinguishing between eternal possibility and possibility at each moment.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not require eternal existence; al-Ghazali exposes a logical confusion.

The philosophers propose that if the world was always possible, it must have always existed. This seems plausible, but al-Ghazali points out a subtle confusion between possibility at every moment and possibility as a timeless property. He uses an analogy from spatial magnitude to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Carefully read the first part of the passage where the philosophers present their reasoning. Notice the logical chain: possibility is eternal, therefore the world must be eternal.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument hinges on the claim that possibility cannot have a beginning. Ask yourself: why do they think that?
3
Examine al-Ghazali's objection
Read the objection starting with 'الاعتراض'. Notice how he distinguishes between eternal possibility and possibility at each moment.
4
Analyze the spatial analogy
Al-Ghazali compares temporal possibility to spatial increase. Both are endless in potential but cannot be actualized infinitely. See the parallel.
5
Summarize the refutation
Conclude why the argument fails: possibility at every moment does not compel actual existence at every moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث لهم على قدم العالم
تمسكوا بأن قالوا : وجود العالم ممكن قبل وجوده ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون ممتنعاً ثم يصير ممكناً ، وهذا الإمكان لا أول له ، أى لم يزل ثابتاً ، ولم يزل العالم ممكناً وجوده ، إذ لا حال من الأحوال يمكن أن يوصف العالم فيه بأنه ممتنع الوجود ، فإذا كان الإمكان لم يزل ، فالممكن على وفق الإمكان أيضاً لم يزل ، فإن معنى قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده ، أنه ليس محالاً وجوده ، فإذا كان ممكناً وجوده أبداً ، لم يكن محالا وجوده أبداً ، وإلا فإن كان محالا وجوده أبداً ، بطل قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده أبداً ، وإن بطل قولنا إنه ممكن وجوده أبداً ، بطل قولنا إن الإمكان لم يزل ، و إن بطل قولنا إن الإمكان لم يزل ، صح قولنا إن الإمكان له أن له أولاً ، كان قبل ذلك غير ممكن ، فيؤدى إلى إثبات أول ، فإذا صح حال لم يكن العالم ممكناً ، ولا كان الله عليه قادراً
الاعتراض : أن يقال : العالم لم يزل ممكن الحدوث ، فلا جرم ما من وقت إلا و يتصور إحداثه فيه ، وإذا قدر موجوداً أبداً لم يكن حادثاً ، فلم يكن الواقع على وفق الإمكان بل على خلافه ، وهذا كقولهم في المكان ، وهو أن تقدير العالم أكبر مما هو ، أو خلق جسم فوق العالم ، ممكن ، وكذا آخر فوق ذلك الآخر ، وهكذا إلى غير نهاية ، فلا نهاية لإمكان الزيادة ، ومع ذلك فوجود ملاء مطلق لا نهاية له ، غير ممكن ، فكذلك وجود لا ينتهى طرفه غير ممكن ، بل كما يقال : الممكن جسم متناهى السطح متناهى السطح ، ولكن لا تتعين مقاديره في الكبر والصغر ، فكذلك الممكن الحدوث ، ومبادئ الوجود لا تتعين في التقدم والتأخر ، وأصل كونه حادثاً متعين ، فإنه الممكن لا غير
A third proof of theirs for the eternity of the world. They held and said: The existence of the world was possible before its existence, for it is impossible that it was impossible [and] then became possible. And this possibility has no beginning, i.e., it has always been established, and the world has always been possible in existence, for there is no state in which the world can be described as impossible of existence. So if possibility never ceased, then the possible in accordance with possibility also never ceased. For the meaning of our saying that its existence is possible is that its existence is not impossible. So if its existence is possible always, then its existence is not impossible always. Otherwise, if its existence were impossible always, then our saying that its existence is possible always would be false. And if our saying that its existence is possible always is false, then our saying that possibility never ceased is false. And if our saying that possibility never ceased is false, then our saying that possibility has a first becomes true, [meaning] that before that it was not possible, leading to establishing a first. So if a state is established in which the world was not possible, then God was not powerful over it.
The objection: It is said: The world has never ceased to be possible of origination; hence there is no time in which its origination is not conceivable. But if it is supposed to exist forever, it would not be originated. So what occurs does not correspond to possibility but rather opposes it. This is like their statement concerning space, namely that the estimation of the world as larger than it is, or the creation of a body above the world, is possible, and likewise another above that other, and so on ad infinitum. So there is no end to the possibility of increase, and yet the existence of an absolutely infinite plenum is not possible. Similarly, the existence of that whose extent does not terminate is not possible. Rather, just as it is said: The possible is a body with a finite surface, but its magnitudes in greatness and smallness are not determined. Likewise, the possible of origination – the principles of existence are not determined in priority and posteriority. The essence of its being originated is determined, for it is the possible and nothing else.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' third argument for the eternity of the world and then al-Ghazali's objection. The argument proceeds by noting that the world's existence was possible before it existed. This possibility cannot have a beginning; otherwise, the world would have been impossible at some point, which is absurd. Therefore, possibility is eternal, and since the world was always possible, it must have always existed (since if it were possible at all times, its existence cannot be impossible at any time, so it must be actual). The argument concludes that if the world had not been possible at some time, God would not have been able to create it then. Al-Ghazali objects by distinguishing between eternal possibility and possibility at each moment. He uses the analogy of spatial magnitude: it is always possible to conceive a larger body, but actual infinite extension is impossible. Similarly, temporal possibility: the world is possible at every moment, but that does not require it to be actually eternal. The objection is that the world's being always possible does not entail its being always existent; rather, it entails that it could be created at any time. The analogy shows that eternal possibility of origination does not imply eternal actualization, just as infinite possible increase in space does not imply an actual infinite body.

Key Terms
إمكان (possibility)
ممكن (possible)
ممتنع (impossible)
حادث (originated)
قدم (eternity)
مكان (space)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument is a valid proof for the eternity of the world.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows it confuses eternal possibility with possibility at each moment; eternal possibility does not require actual existence.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Ghazali use to refute the third argument for the eternity of the world?
A) Analogy of a body moving in space
B) Analogy of the possibility of increase in spatial magnitude
C) Analogy of a thing being impossible
D) Analogy of the universe expanding

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali refutes the philosophers' third argument by showing that eternal possibility does not entail eternal actual existence, using the analogy of infinite spatial extension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you believe something is 'possible' for Allah, do you actually expect it to happen? Or do you treat possibility as just a theoretical idea? What is one thing you have been too afraid to ask of Allah because you doubt it is possible for you? Write it down and make du'a for it today.
?
The philosophers assumed that what is always possible must always exist. Are there good deeds you keep postponing because you think they will always be possible later? What is one specific action you will take before the day ends, without further delay?
?
Al-Ghazali warns that eternal possibility does not guarantee actual occurrence. Have you been relying on the possibility of future repentance to delay turning back to Allah? Will you repent today, right now, from a specific sin—not just promise, but actually repent?

The Fourth Argument: Possibility Requires Pre-existent Matter
Presents the philosophers' fourth argument for the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the possibility of originated things requires a pre-existing matter, and why they conclude matter is eternal.

Core Idea
Possibility is a relational attribute that cannot exist without a material substrate.

This lesson examines the fourth argument for the eternity of the world presented by the philosophers. They argue that every originated thing must have matter that precedes it, because the possibility of its existence is a relational attribute requiring a substrate. This leads to the conclusion that matter itself cannot be originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then its translation. Notice how the argument is structured step by step.
The passage is incomplete due to a page break; focus on the available text.
2
Classify the possible, impossible, necessary
Identify the threefold classification of any thing before existence. The argument uses this to establish that the world is possible.
This is a standard philosophical trichotomy.
3
Understand possibility as relational
The argument claims possibility is a relational attribute that must inhere in a substrate. The only suitable substrate is matter.
This is the core move.
4
Follow why matter cannot be originated
If matter were originated, its own possibility would require another matter, leading to infinite regress. Therefore matter is eternal.
This is the key conclusion.
5
Distinguish possibility from being an object of power
The argument rejects that 'possible' means the same as 'object of power' (m-qud-r). They are distinct concepts; otherwise it would be circular.
Also notes that possibility is not reducible to divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل رابع) وهو أنهم قالوا : كل حادث فالمادة التي فيه تسبقه ، إذ لا يستغنى الحادث مادة فلا تكون المادة حادثة ، وإنما الحادث الصور والأعراض والكيفيات عن لا الطارئة على المواد . وبيانه أن كل حادث فهو قبل حدوثه لا يخلو : إما أن يكون ممكن الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود ، أو واجب الوجود ، ومحال أن يكون ممتنعاً ، لأن الممتنع في ذاته لا يوجد قط ، ومحال أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، فإن الواجب لذاته يعلم قط ، فدل على أنه ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فإذن إمكان الوجود حاصل له قبل وجوده ، و إمكان الوجود وصف إضافى ، لا قوام له بنفسه ، فلابد له من محل يضاف إليه ، ولا محل إلا المادة ، فيضاف إليها ، كما يقال : هذه المادة قابلة للحرارة والبرودة ، أو السواد والبياض ، أو الحركة والسكون ، أى ممكن لها حدوث هذه الكيفيات وطريان هذه التغيرات. فيكون الإمكان وصفاً للمادة ، والمادة لا يكون لها مادة ، فلا يمكن أن تحدث ، إذ لو حدثت لكان إمكان وجودها سابقاً على وجودها ، وكان الإمكان قائماً بنفسه ، غير مضاف إلى شيء، أنه وصف إضافي لا يعقل قائماً بنفسه ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن معنى مع الإمكان إلى كونه مقدوراً ، وكون القديم قادراً عليه ، لأنا لا نعرف كون يرجع الشيء مقدوراً إلا بكونه ممكناً ، فنقول هو مقدور ، لأنه ممكن ، وليس بمقدور لأنه ليس بممكن ، فإن كان قولنا هو ممكن إلى أنه أنه مقدور ، فكأنا قلنا : هو مقدور لأنه مقدور ، وليس بمقدور لأنه ليس بمقدور ، وهو تعريف الشيء بنفسه ، فدل أن كونه ممكناً قضية أخرى في العقل ظاهرة بها تعرف القضية الثانية ، وهى كونه مقدوراً ، ويستحيل أن يرجع ذلك إلى علم القديم بكونه ممكناً ، فإن العلم يستدعى معلوماً ، فالإمكان المعلوم غير العلم
يرجع
A fourth proof: They said: Every originated thing has matter that precedes it, for the originated thing is not independent of matter. So matter is not originated; rather the originated things are the forms, accidents, and qualities that [؟] occur to matters. Its explanation: Every originated thing, before its origination, is either possible of existence, impossible of existence, or necessary of existence. It is impossible that it be impossible, because the impossible in itself never exists. And it is impossible that it be necessary of existence in itself, because that which is necessary in itself [never ceases?] – we know it is not such. So it indicates that it is possible of existence in itself. Therefore, possibility of existence obtains for it before its existence. And possibility of existence is a relational attribute, having no subsistence by itself. So it must have a substrate to which it is related, and there is no substrate except matter, so it is related to it. As it is said: This matter is receptive to heat and cold, or blackness and whiteness, or motion and rest, i.e., it is possible for these qualities to occur and for these changes to come upon it. So possibility is an attribute of matter. And matter has no matter, so it cannot originate. For if it were to originate, its possibility of existence would be prior to its existence, and possibility would subsist by itself, not related to anything, but it is a relational attribute not intelligible as subsisting by itself. And it cannot be said that the meaning of possibility is just that it is an object of power and that the Eternal is powerful over it, because we do not know that a thing is an object of power except by its being possible. So we say it is an object of power because it is possible, and it is not an object of power because it is not possible. If our saying that it is possible is [the same as] that it is an object of power, then we would have said: It is an object of power because it is an object of power, and not an object of power because it is not an object of power, which is defining a thing by itself. So it indicates that its being possible is another proposition in the mind, by which the second proposition (its being an object of power) is known. And it is impossible that this be reduced to the Eternal's knowledge of its being possible, for knowledge requires a known object; the known possibility is not knowledge.
[passage cut off]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fourth argument for the eternity of the world, which is based on the concept of matter as a necessary substrate for possibility. The argument states that every originated thing requires matter that precedes it, so matter itself cannot be originated. The explanation begins by classifying every thing before existence as either possible, impossible, or necessary. Since the world is not impossible (it exists) and not necessary (otherwise it would always exist necessarily), it is possible. This possibility is a relational attribute that must inhere in a substrate, and the only suitable substrate is matter. Therefore, matter must pre-exist and cannot be originated (otherwise its own possibility would need another matter, leading to an infinite regress). The argument then discusses the relationship between possibility and being an object of power (مقدور). It argues that possibility is not identical to being an object of power; rather, possibility is a distinct concept that grounds the judgment of being an object of power. If they were the same, it would be a circular definition. The argument also denies that 'being possible' reduces to God's knowledge of it as possible, because knowledge requires a known object distinct from the knowledge itself. The passage is incomplete, ending mid-argument on the page.

Key Terms
حادث
مادة
إمكان
وصف إضافى
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'possible' means the same as 'being an object of divine power'.
Correction: The argument shows they are distinct: we know something is an object of power because it is possible, not the other way around. Identifying them leads to circular reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does the possibility of an event require before its existence?
A) The power of an agent
B) A material substrate (matter)
C) The knowledge of God
D) The event itself

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that possibility is a real relational attribute that must inhere in matter, making matter eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument claims matter is eternal and has always been a substrate for possibility. If this were true, what would that mean for your understanding of God's absolute independence and His ability to create from nothing? How does your heart respond to the idea that something other than God could be eternal?
?
The philosophers distinguish possibility from being an object of power. Do you ever treat God's power as limited by what is 'possible' in your own mind, as if possibility were something outside God that He must work with? Reflect on a situation where you doubted God's power because something seemed impossible. What does this lesson say about possibility being distinct from power?
?
If possibility requires matter, then the possibility of your own existence depends on pre-existing matter. How does that challenge or affirm your belief that you exist solely because of God's will and power? Are you willing to accept that your very possibility as a creature is not independent but rooted in God's creation of matter?

The Fourth Proof: Matter Precedes Every Originated Thing
Ghazali presents the philosophers' fourth proof for the eternity of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that every originated thing requires pre-existing matter, and why matter itself cannot be originated.

Core Idea
Possibility is a relative attribute that needs a substrate—matter—so matter is eternal.

The philosophers argue that every originated thing is preceded by matter, because the possibility of its existence is a relative attribute that must inhere in a substrate. This substrate is matter, and since matter has no matter of its own, it cannot be originated. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, including responses to objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation to get the full argument.
Notice the logical structure: statement, explanation, refutation of alternatives.
2
Identify the three categories of things before existence
The passage says every originated thing before its existence is either possible, impossible, or necessary. Understand why impossible and necessary are ruled out.
This is a standard kalam taxonomy.
3
Understand the nature of possibility as a relative attribute
Possibility (imkān) is described as a relative attribute (waṣf iḍāfī). Grasp why it needs a substrate and why that substrate must be matter.
Relative attributes do not subsist by themselves.
4
Follow the argument that matter cannot be originated
If matter were originated, its possibility would need another matter—an infinite regress. Therefore, matter must be eternal.
This is the core conclusion.
5
Reflect on the refutation of alternative interpretations
The passage rejects that possibility means 'being predestined' or 'known by God.' See how those lead to circularity or require an object distinct from knowledge.
This strengthens the argument for matter as substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل رابع) وهو أنهم قالوا : كل حادث فالمادة التي فيه تسبقه ، إذ لا يستغنى الحادث عن مادة فلا تكون المادة حادثة ، وإنما الحادث الصور والأعراض والكيفيات الطارئة على المواد . وبيانه أن كل حادث فهو قبل حدوثه لا يخلو : إما أن يكون ممكن الوجود أو ممتنع الوجود ، أو واجب الوجود ، ومحال أن يكون ممتنعاً ، لأن الممتنع في ذاته لا يوجد قط ، ومحال أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ، فإن الواجب لذاته لا يتغير ولا يحدث ، فدل على أنه ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فإذن إمكان الوجود حاصل له قبل وجوده ، و إمكان الوجود وصف إضافى ، لا قوام له بنفسه ، فلابد له من محل يضاف إليه ، ولا محل إلا المادة ، فيضاف إليها ، كما يقال : هذه المادة قابلة للحرارة والبرودة ، أو السواد والبياض ، أو الحركة والسكون ، أى ممكن لها حدوث هذه الكيفيات وطريان هذه التغيرات. فيكون الإمكان وصفاً للمادة ، والمادة لا يكون لها مادة ، فلا يمكن أن تحدث ، إذ لو حدثت لكان إمكان وجودها سابقاً على وجودها ، وكان الإمكان قائماً بنفسه ، غير مضاف إلى شيء، وأنه وصف إضافي لا يعقل قائماً بنفسه ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن معنى الإمكان يرجع إلى كونه مقدوراً ، وكون القديم قادراً عليه ، لأنا لا نعرف كون الشيء مقدوراً إلا بكونه ممكناً ، فنقول هو مقدور ، لأنه ممكن ، وليس بمقدور لأنه ليس بممكن ، فإن كان قولنا هو ممكن يرجع إلى أنه مقدور ، فكأنا قلنا : هو مقدور لأنه مقدور ، وليس بمقدور لأنه ليس بمقدور ، وهو تعريف الشيء بنفسه ، فدل أن كونه ممكناً قضية أخرى في العقل ظاهرة بها تعرف القضية الثانية ، وهى كونه مقدوراً ، ويستحيل أن يرجع ذلك إلى علم القديم بكونه ممكناً ، فإن العلم يستدعى معلوماً ، فالإمكان المعلوم غير العلم لا محالة ، ثم هو وصف إضافى ، فلابد من ذات يضاف إليها ، وليس إلا المادة ، فكل حادث فقد سبقه مادة ، فلم تكن المادة الأولى حادثة بحال.
Fourth proof: They said: Every originated thing is preceded by the matter in it, since the originated thing is not independent of matter, so matter is not originated. Rather, the originated things are the forms, accidents, and qualities that occur to matters. Its explanation: Every originated thing before its occurrence is either possible of existence, impossible of existence, or necessary of existence. It cannot be impossible because the impossible in itself never exists. It cannot be necessary of existence in itself, for that which is necessary in itself does not change and does not occur. So it is proven that it is possible of existence in itself. Therefore, possibility of existence belongs to it before its existence. Possibility of existence is a relative attribute, subsisting not by itself, so it must have a substrate to which it is attributed. There is no substrate except matter, so it is attributed to it, as it is said: This matter is receptive to heat and cold, or blackness and whiteness, or motion and rest, i.e., it is possible for these qualities to occur and these changes to arise. Thus possibility is an attribute of matter, and matter has no matter, so it cannot occur, for if it occurred, the possibility of its existence would precede its existence, and the possibility would subsist by itself, not attributed to anything, while it is a relative attribute not conceivable as subsisting by itself. Nor can it be said that the meaning of possibility refers to its being predestined and the eternal being powerful over it, for we do not know something to be predestined except by its being possible. We say: It is predestined because it is possible, and it is not predestined because it is not possible. If our saying 'it is possible' refers to its being predestined, then we would have said: It is predestined because it is predestined, and not predestined because it is not predestined – which is defining a thing by itself. So it is proven that its being possible is another rational judgment, apparent, by which the second judgment – its being predestined – is known. And it is impossible for that to refer to the eternal's knowledge of its being possible, for knowledge requires a known object. The known possibility is other than knowledge necessarily. Then it is a relative attribute, so there must be a substrate to which it is attributed, and that is only matter. Thus every originated thing is preceded by matter, so the first matter is not originated at all.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fourth proof for the eternity of matter. The argument begins by asserting that every originated thing (ḥādith) is preceded by the matter in which it occurs, because the originated thing cannot be independent of matter. Thus matter itself cannot be originated; what originates are the forms, accidents, and qualities that arise in matter. The proof then explains the nature of an originated thing before its existence: it must be either possible, impossible, or necessary. It cannot be impossible or necessary, so it must be possible. This possibility (imkān) is a relative attribute (waṣf iḍāfī), which cannot subsist by itself; it requires a substrate (maḥall) to which it is attributed. That substrate is matter. Therefore, possibility is an attribute of matter, and since matter has no matter, it cannot itself come into existence. If matter were to arise, its possibility would precede it, and that possibility would subsist without a substrate, which is impossible. The text then refutes two alternative interpretations of possibility: that it means being predestined (maqdūr) by the eternal power, or that it refers to divine knowledge. The first leads to circularity; the second requires an object of knowledge distinct from knowledge. So possibility remains a relative attribute requiring matter. Hence every originated thing has pre-existing matter, so the first matter is unoriginated.

إمكان
حادث
مادة
وصف إضافي
ممكن الوجود
واجب الوجود
ممتنع الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possibility is a real entity that can exist independently without a substrate.
Correction: The passage argues that possibility is a relative attribute (waṣf iḍāfī) that cannot subsist by itself; it requires a substrate, which is matter.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' fourth proof, why can't possibility itself be a self-subsisting attribute?
A) Because possibility is an accident that cannot exist alone.
B) Because the philosophers define it as a relative attribute that needs something to be attributed to.
C) Because possibility is identical to divine knowledge.
D) Because possibility is a mental construct only.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that every originated thing requires pre-existing matter because its possibility is a relative attribute that can only inhere in matter, making matter itself unoriginated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The philosophers argued that possibility is not something that stands alone—it needs a foundation. In your life, where have you placed your hopes and possibilities on things that are themselves unstable? What would it mean to anchor your possibilities on the Eternal, rather than on the created?
?
If matter is eternal as the philosophers claim, then your very existence is tied to something that has always been there. Does this make you feel small? Or does it make you wonder about the One who sustains this matter? How does your reliance on material things distract you from the immaterial Reality that gives them existence?
?
The proof rests on the idea that every originated thing has a cause and a substrate. But have you ever lived as if your own existence is self-grounded—as if you owe nothing to anyone? When you look at the matter of your body, the air in your lungs, can you truly say that you have thanked the One who brought them into being every moment?

The Objection to Possibility as a Real Attribute
Objector argues possibility is a rational judgment with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that possibility is a rational judgment, not a real attribute, and the three proofs supporting this.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental judgment, not a real attribute needing a substrate.

The objector counters the fourth proof by arguing that possibility (imkān) is simply a rational judgment, not a real quality of things. Whatever the mind can conceive without contradiction is called possible; whatever contradicts is impossible; whatever cannot be imagined as nonexistent is necessary. To support this, the objector presents three proofs that show possibility does not require an existent substrate. This passage is crucial because it challenges the very foundation of the philosophers' proof of the world's pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the opening lines where the objector defines possibility as a rational judgment.
Notice the three categories: possible, impossible, and necessary are all mind-dependent.
2
Study the three proofs
Examine each proof carefully, noting how each undermines the need for a substrate.
The first proof uses impossibility, the second uses blackness/whiteness, the third uses human souls.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this objection turns the philosophers' own views against them.
If souls are immaterial yet have possibility, then possibility does not require matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض أن يقال : الإمكان الذي ذكرتموه يرجع إلى قضاء العقل ، فكل ما قدر العقل وجوده فلم يمتنع عليه تقديره ، سميناه ممكناً و إن امتنع سميناه مستحيلا ، وإن لم يقد، على تقدير عدمه ، سميناه واجباً ، فهذه قضايا عقلية لا تحتاج إلى موجود حتى تجعل وصفاً له بدليل ثلاثة أمور . أحدها: أن الإمكان لو استدعى شيئاً موجوداً يضاف إليه ويقال : إنه إمكانه ، لاستدعى الامتناع شيئاً موجوداً يقال إنه امتناعه ، وليس للمتنع في ذاته وجود ، ولا مادة يطرأ عليها المحال حتى يضاف الامتناع إلى المادة . والثانى : أن السواد والبياض يقضى العقل فيهما قبل وجودهما بكونهما ممكنين، فإن كان هذا الإمكان مضافاً إلى الجسم الذي يطرآن عليه حتى يقال : معناه أن هذا الجسم يمكن أن يسود وأن يبيض ، فإذن ليس البياض في نفسه ممكناً ولا له نعت الإمكان ، وإنما الممكن الجسم والإمكان مضاف إليه ، فنقول : ما حكم نفس السواد في ذاته ، أهو ممكن ، أو واجب ، أو ممتنع ؟ ولابد من القول بأنه ممكن . فدل أن العقل فى القضية بالإمكان ، لا يفتقر إلى وضع ذات موجودة يضيف إليها الإمكان . والثالث : أن نفوس الآدميين عندهم جواهر قائمة بأنفسها ، ليست بجسم ولا مادة ، ولا منطبعة فى مادة ، وهى حادثة على ما اختاره ابن سينا والمحققون منهم ، ولها إمكان قبل حدوثها ، وليس لها ذات ولا مادة ، فإمكانها وصف إضافى ، ولا يرجع إلى قدرة القادر ، ولا إلى علمه بكونه ممكناً فإلى ماذا يرجع ؟ فينقلب عليهم هذا الإشكال.
The objection: It is said: The possibility you mentioned refers to the judgment of the intellect. For everything that the intellect estimates its existence and does not find impossible in its estimation, we call it possible. If it finds it impossible, we call it impossible. If it cannot estimate its non-existence, we call it necessary. These are rational judgments that do not require an existent to be described by them, as evidenced by three things. First: If possibility required something existent to which it is attributed and it is said to be its possibility, then impossibility would similarly require something existent to which it is attributed and it is said to be its impossibility. But the impossible in itself has no existence, nor is there matter upon which the impossible occurs so that impossibility could be attributed to matter. Second: The intellect judges blackness and whiteness before their existence as possible. If this possibility is attributed to the body in which they occur, and it is said that its meaning is that this body can become black or white, then blackness itself is not possible and does not have the attribute of possibility; rather, the body is possible and possibility is attributed to it. So we say: What is the judgment of blackness itself in its essence? Is it possible, necessary, or impossible? It must be said that it is possible. Thus the intellect in judging possibility does not require the positing of an existent essence to which possibility is attributed. Third: Human souls, according to them, are self-subsistent substances, not bodies, not matter, and not impressed in matter. They are originated, according to Ibn Sina and the investigators among them. They have possibility before their occurrence, yet they have no essence or matter. So their possibility is a relative attribute, and it does not refer to the power of the powerful, nor to His knowledge of their being possible. To what then does it refer? This objection turns back upon them.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the objector's response to the fourth proof. The objector argues that possibility (imkān) is not a real attribute requiring a substrate; rather, it is merely a judgment of the intellect (qāḍā' al-'aql). Whatever the intellect can conceive as existing without contradiction is called possible; whatever is contradictory is impossible; whatever cannot be conceived as nonexistent is necessary. These are rational categories, not real properties. The objector provides three proofs. First, if possibility required an existent substrate, then impossibility would likewise require one, but the impossible has no existence and no matter, so impossibility cannot be attributed to anything. Second, consider blackness and whiteness: before they exist, the intellect judges them possible. If that possibility is attributed to the body, then blackness itself is not possible; yet we still ask whether blackness in itself is possible, and we must say it is possible. Thus the intellect does not need a real substrate. Third, human souls, according to Ibn Sina, are incorporeal substances without matter, yet they are originated and have possibility before existing. Since they have no matter, their possibility cannot be attributed to a substrate, nor can it be reduced to divine power or knowledge. Therefore the philosophers' own view implies that possibility does not need matter, and their proof is turned against them.

جواهر قائمة بأنفسها

Correction: The objector demonstrates that possibility is merely a rational judgment, as shown by the impossibility-proof (first proof), the blackness/whiteness-proof (second), and the soul-proof (third).

Key Takeaway

?
The objection shows that impossibility does not require a substrate because the impossible does not exist. Think of sins or actions you consider impossible for you to commit—are you using that judgment as a false security? Blackness Depends on a Substrate
Ghazali refutes the claim that blackness is possible in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that blackness cannot be possible in itself and how it depends on a substrate for its existence.

Core Idea
Blackness has no independent existence; its possibility depends entirely on a body prepared for change.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers by examining the claim that blackness is possible in itself. He argues that this is a mistake because any accident like blackness requires a substrate to exist. Without a body, blackness is impossible, not possible. Let's read his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to get a feel for Ghazali's argument.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Notice that Ghazali considers two cases: blackness taken in isolation, and blackness as a state in a body. See how he judges the possibility in each.
The key is the substrate (majall).
3
Reflect on the implication
Understand that possibility belongs to the body's capacity for change, not to the accident itself. This refutes the idea that blackness has its own essence that could be possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثانى ، وهو كون السواد فى نفسه ممكناً ، فغلط ، فإنه إن خذ مجرداً دون مجل يحله ، كان ممتنعاً لا ممكناً ، وإنما يصير ممكناً إذا قدر هيأة في الجسم مهيأ لتبدل هيأة ، والتبدل ممكن على الجسم ، و إلا فليس للسواد نفس مفردة ، حتى توصف بإمكان
As for the second point, namely that blackness in itself is possible, it is a mistake. For if it is taken in isolation without a substrate to inhere in, it would be impossible, not possible. It only becomes possible if it is considered as a state in a body prepared for the change of state, and change is possible for the body. Otherwise, blackness has no single essence such that it can be described with possibility.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that blackness (al-sawad) is possible in itself. Ghazali argues that this is a mistake because blackness cannot exist independently; it must inhere in a substrate (a body). If taken in isolation without a substrate, it would be impossible, not possible. Blackness only becomes possible when considered as a state (hay'a) in a body that is prepared for a change of state. The change itself is possible for the body, but blackness has no separate essence (nafs mufrada) to which possibility can be attributed. Thus possibility is not intrinsic to blackness but belongs to the body's capacity for change.

الممتنع
الهيأة

Misconception: Blackness has its own essence that can be described as possible independently of any substrate.
Correction: Ghazali shows that blackness has no single essence (nafs mufrada) and only becomes possible when considered as a state in a body prepared for change.

Checkpoint

The Possibility of the Soul Is Relational
Ghazali evaluates three positions on the soul's possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali demonstrates the soul's possibility is always relative to matter, regardless of whether the soul is eternal or created, impressed or not.

Core Idea
The soul's possibility is never intrinsic, always relative to matter.

Ghazali examines several philosophical positions on the soul's origin and nature. Strikingly, he finds a common thread: whether the soul is eternal or temporally originated, whether it is impressed in matter or merely related as a governor, its possibility is never an independent property but always depends on matter. This passage shows how he traces possibility back to matter in every case.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the continuous Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the structure of different views emerge naturally.
2
Identify the three views on the soul
The passage presents three distinct positions: (1) soul eternal but attachable, (2) soul created and impressed in matter (Galen), (3) soul created but not impressed, yet related as governor. Find each one.
They are introduced by 'وأما الثالث' (the third point) and then contrasted.
3
See how each view ties possibility to matter
For each view, notice how Ghazali links the soul's possibility to matter. In the first, possibility attaches via attachment. In the second, via impression. In the third, via governance. The key is that in all cases, possibility is not intrinsic to the soul itself.
The phrase 'فالإمكان راجع إليها' appears at the end, summarizing the conclusion.
4
Understand Ghazali's conclusion
Ghazali does not argue for one view over another. Instead, he shows that regardless of which view one holds, the soul's possibility is always relational to matter. This undermines any claim that the soul has intrinsic possibility.
This is a dialectical move in the Tahafut: showing that the opponent's own views lead to a different conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث ، وهو النفس فهي قديمة عند فريق ، ولكن ممكن لها التعلق بالأبدان ، فلم يلزم على هذا ما قلتم . ومن سلم حدوثه ، فقد اعتقد فريق منهم منطبع في المادة تابع للمزاج على ما دل عليه كلام « جالينوس » فى بعض المواضع ، فتكون فى مادة ، وإمكانها مضاف إلى مادتها ، وعلى مذهب من سلم أنها حادثة وليست منطبعة ، فمعناه أن المادة ممكن لها أن يدبرها نفس ناطقة ، فيكون الإمكان السابق على الحدوث مضافاً إلى المادة، فإنها وإن لم تنطبع فيها فلها علاقة معها ، إذ هى المدبرة المستعملة لها، فيكون الإمكان راجعاً إليها بهذا الطريق
As for the third point—namely the soul—it is eternal according to one group, but it is possible for it to be attached to bodies, so what you said does not follow. For those who affirm its temporal origination, one group believes it is impressed in matter, following the temperament, as indicated by Galen's words in some places; thus it is in matter, and its possibility is relative to its matter. According to the doctrine of those who affirm its temporal origination and that it is not impressed, the meaning is that matter has the possibility of being governed by an intellective soul; thus the possibility prior to origination is relative to the matter. For even if it is not impressed in matter, it has a relationship with it, since it is the governor and user of it; so possibility reverts to matter in this way.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the possibility of the soul (al-nafs). Ghazali presents different philosophical views. First, some hold the soul is eternal but can be attached to bodies; in that case, its possibility is not intrinsic but relative to attachment. Second, among those who hold the soul is temporally originated, some (like Galen) believe it is impressed in matter and follows the temperament; thus its possibility is relative to its matter. Third, another group holds the soul is originated but not impressed; in this view, matter has the possibility of being governed by an intellective soul, so the possibility of the soul's existence is again relative to matter because of the soul's relationship as governor and user of the body. In all cases, the possibility of the soul is not an intrinsic attribute of the soul itself but is relational to matter.

Key Terms
النفس
الإمكان
المادة
منطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul has intrinsic possibility independent of matter.
Correction: Ghazali shows that whether eternal or created, impressed or not, the soul's possibility always depends on matter—either through attachment, impression, or governance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common factor in the different views on the soul's possibility?
A) The soul is eternal.
B) The soul is impossible.
C) The soul's possibility is relative to matter.
D) The soul is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of the soul is never an independent quality; it is always relational to matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul's very possibility is not something you own—it is given through its relation to your body and its governance. How does that reshape your understanding of your utter dependence on Allah in every moment? What does that demand from you today?
?
If the soul's possibility is always relative to matter, then your existence as a soul is tied to your body and its actions. Will you live today as if your soul's governance of your body truly matters for eternity? What one change can you make right now?

Possibility Attributed to the Agent's Power
Ghazali refutes the substrate excuse and redefines possibility as agent-relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' claim that possibility requires a material substrate and his alternative that possibility is a relation to the agent's power.

Core Idea
Possibility can be attributed to the agent's power, not just a substrate.

In this passage, Ghazali challenges the philosophers' argument that blackness and whiteness cannot be possible because they have no independent essence. He first shows that the intellect still conceives of them as possible essences. Then he introduces created souls as a stronger counterexample: they have possibility before existing without any substrate. Finally, he proposes that possibility is simply the relation of the agent's power to bring about the thing, regardless of imprinting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow of refutation and proposal.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Find where Ghazali refutes the excuse about black and white, and where he gives his alternative attribution of possibility.
3
Understand the soul example
Why does Ghazali use originated souls as a counterexample? They have possibility before existing but no substrate.
4
See the agent-relative possibility
Ghazali says possibility is that the powerful agent can bring it about—a relation to the agent, not a substrate.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument challenges the philosophers' framework: possibility is relational, not inherent in matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العذر عن السواد والبياض بأنه لا نفس له ولا ذات منفردة، إن عنى بذلك في الوجود فنعم، وإن عنى بذلك في العقل فلا، فإن العقل يعقل السواد الكلى، ويحكم عليه بالإمكان في ذاته. ثم إن العذر باطل بالنفوس الحادثة، فإن لها ذواتاً مفردة وإمكاناً سابقاً على الحدوث، وليس ثمت ما يضاف إليه. وقولهم: إن المادة ممكن لها أن تدبرها النفوس، فهذه إضافات بعيدة، فإن اكتفيتم بهذا فلا يبعد أن يقال: معنى إمكان الحادث أن القادر عليه يمكن في حقه أن يحدثه، فتكون إضافة إلى الفاعل مع أنه ليس منطبعاً فيه، كما أنه إضافة إلى البدن المنفعل مع أنه لا تنطبع فيه، ولا فرق بين النسبة إلى الفاعل والنسبة إلى المنفعل إذا لم يكن انطباع في الموضعين.
As for the excuse regarding blackness and whiteness—that they have no self nor independent essence—if this is meant with respect to existence, then yes; but if it is meant with respect to the intellect, then no. For the intellect understands universal blackness and judges that it is possible in its essence. Moreover, the excuse is refuted by the case of originated souls, for they have independent essences and possibility prior to their origination, yet there is nothing to which it is attributed. And their saying: 'Matter is such that souls can manage it'—these are far-fetched attributions. If you are satisfied with this, then it is not far-fetched to say: The meaning of the possibility of an originated thing is that the powerful agent can bring it about, so it is an attribution to the agent, even though it is not imprinted in him, just as it is an attribution to the passive body even though it is not imprinted in it. And there is no difference between the relation to the agent and the relation to the patient when there is no imprinting in either case.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage tackles the philosophers' claim that blackness and whiteness have no independent essence, so their possibility cannot be attached to anything. Ghazali counters: in the mind, universal blackness is an essence and is judged possible. Then he introduces the example of created souls: they have independent essences and possibility before they exist, with no material substrate. If the philosophers try to attribute possibility to matter through the soul's relation, Ghazali dismisses it as far-fetched. He offers an alternative: possibility can be attributed to the agent's power (qudra) to bring about the thing, without any requirement of imprinting. This is a key argument that possibility is a relational concept, not a property of a substrate.

Key Terms
سواد (sawad)
بياض (bayad)
نفس (nafs)
ذات (dhat)
النفوس الحادثة (al-nufus al-haditha)
قادر (qadir)
انطباع (intiba')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must inhere in a material substrate.
Correction: Possibility can be a relational concept, attributed to the agent's power to bring about the thing.

Checkpoint
What is the point of Ghazali's example of originated souls?
A) Souls are eternal.
B) Souls have possibility before existence without a substrate.
C) Souls are not essences.
D) Souls are imprinted in matter.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a relation to the agent's power, not a property of a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though every possibility in your life depends directly on God's power, or do you functionally attribute possibilities to material conditions and secondary causes? Where specifically today did you assume a situation was impossible or possible based on worldly factors rather than God's will?
?
Ghazali uses the example of souls—your own soul—as something that had possibility before it existed, without needing a material substrate. If your very existence depended solely on God's power and not on any pre-existing matter, how should that change the way you view your dependence on God right now?
?
If possibility is just a relation to God's power, then every moment you exist is because God is actively bringing you about. Does your heart feel that immediate dependence, or do you act as if your existence is a fixed fact that no longer requires divine support? What would it mean to wake up tomorrow and truly believe that your continued existence is a new act of God's power?

Philosophers' Three Proofs for Future Eternity
Three arguments for future eternity presented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key arguments that philosophers use to claim the world will never end, based on causality, the nature of non-existence, and continuous possibility.

Core Idea
Philosophers argue the world's future eternity follows from its past eternity and the cause-effect relationship.

In this part of the Tahafut, Ghazali presents the philosophers' reasoning for the world's everlasting future. He notes that this issue is a direct extension of the previous one on past eternity. The same four proofs apply, but he focuses on three specific arguments. This lesson covers those arguments and their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
First proof: Cause and effect
The philosophers argue that since the cause (God) is eternal, the effect (world) must be eternal in both directions: no beginning and no end.
This argument assumes a necessary relationship between cause and effect.
2
Second proof: Non-existence after existence implies time
If the world ceased, its non-existence would have an 'after', which would require time to exist before that non-existence. Thus the world must be everlasting.
3
Third proof: Possibility is continuous
Since the possibility of existence is always present, the philosopher says existence itself can be continuous.
Ghazali will later critique this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٢] في إبطال قولهم في أبدية العالم والزمان والمكان. ليعلم أن هذه المسألة فرع الأولى، فإن العالم عندهم كما أنه أزلي لا بداية لوجوده، فهو أبدي لا نهاية لآخره، ولا يتصور فساده ولا فناؤه، بل لم يزل كذلك، ولا يزال أيضاً كذلك. وأدلتهم الأربعة التي ذكرناها في الأزلية جارية في الأبدية، والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق. فإنهم يقولون: إن العالم معلول، وعلته أزلية أبدية فكان المعلول مع العلة، ويقولون: إذا لم تتغير العلة لم يتغير المعلول، وعليه بنوا منع الحدوث، وهو بعينه جار في الانقطاع. وهذا مسلكهم الأول. ومسلكهم الثاني: أن العالم إذا عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده، فيكون له (بعد) ففيه إثبات الزمان. ومسلكهم الثالث: أن إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع، فكذلك الوجود الممكن يجوز أن يكون على وفق الإمكان، إلا أن هذا الدليل لا يقوى، فإنا نحيل أن يكون أزلياً، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبدياً، لو أبقاه الله تعالى أبداً، إذ ليس من ضرورة الحادث أن يكون له آخر، ومن ضرورة الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، وأن يكون له أول. ولم يوجب أن يكون للعالم لا محالة آخر إلا أبو الهذيل العلاف، فإنه قال: كما يستحيل في الماضي دورات لا نهاية لها، فكذلك في المستقبل؛ وهو فاسد، لأن كل المستقبل قط لا يدخل في الوجود لا متلاحقاً ولا متساوقاً، والماضي قد دخل كله في الوجود متلاحقاً، وإن لم يكن متساوقاً. وإذ قد تبين أنا لا نحيل بقاء العالم أبداً من حيث العقل، بل نجوز إبقاءه وإفناءه، فإنما يعرف الواقع من قسمي الممكن بالشرع، فلا يتعلق النظر فيه بالعقول.
Issue [2] on refuting their claim about the everlastingness (abadiyya) of the world, time, and space. Know that this issue is a branch of the first, for the world in their view, just as it is pre-eternal (azalī) with no beginning to its existence, so it is everlasting (abadī) with no end to its final term, and its corruption (fasād) and annihilation (fanā') are inconceivable. Rather, it has always been so, and it will always be so. The four proofs that we mentioned concerning pre-eternity (azaliyya) apply to everlastingness (abadiyya), and the objection is the same objection with no difference. For they say: The world is an effect (maʿlūl), and its cause is pre-eternal and everlasting, so the effect coexists with the cause. And they say: If the cause does not change, the effect does not change, and upon this they based the prevention of origination (ḥudūth), and the same applies to cessation (inqiṭāʿ). This is their first way. Their second way: That if the world were to cease to exist, its non-existence would be after its existence, so it would have an 'after' (baʿd), and in that is the affirmation of time. Their third way: That the possibility (imkān) of existence is not cut off, so likewise possible existence is permissible to be in accordance with possibility. However, this proof is not strong, for we deem it impossible for it to be pre-eternal (azalī), but we do not deem it impossible for it to be everlasting (abadī) if God Most High preserved it forever, for it is not necessary for an originated thing (ḥādith) to have an end, and it is necessary for an act (fiʿl) to be originated and to have a beginning. And no one made it necessary that the world must have an end except Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, for he said: Just as it is impossible for past cycles without end, so likewise in the future. This is invalid, because the entire future is not entered into existence at all, neither successively nor synchronously, while the past has all entered into existence successively, even if not synchronously. Since it is clear that we do not deem it impossible for the world to remain forever from the standpoint of reason, but rather we permit its preservation and its annihilation, then what is actual from the two divisions of the possible is known only by the Law (sharʿ), and rational inquiry does not pertain to it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage introduces Issue 2, which is the philosophers' claim that the world is everlasting in the future (abadiyya). Ghazali notes it is a branch of the first issue on past eternity (azaliyya). The same four proofs used for past eternity apply here. He enumerates three of their arguments: (1) The cause-effect relationship: since the cause is eternal, the effect is eternal. (2) If the world ceased, its non-existence would be after existence, implying time. (3) The possibility of existence is continuous, so existence should be continuous. Ghazali weakens the third argument by distinguishing between past and future eternity: we deem past eternity impossible (because no beginning), but we do not deem future eternity impossible if God preserves it forever, because an originated thing does not necessarily have an end. He refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that infinite future cycles are impossible, arguing that the future never fully enters existence, unlike the past. He concludes that reason does not forbid future eternity; the actual outcome is known only from revelation.

Key Terms
أبدية
أزلية
معلول
حدوث
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument for future eternity according to the philosophers?
A) The possibility of existence is continuous.
B) The cause-effect relationship requires the effect to be eternal like the cause.
C) Non-existence after existence implies time.
D) The world is not an originated thing.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use three main arguments to support the world's future eternity: cause-effect, non-existence implying time, and continuous possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue from causality that the world must be everlasting. Do you live as if this world is your ultimate home, or do you acknowledge that it will end? What would change today if you truly believed that only Allah remains?
?
The second proof says that if the world ceased, its non-existence would have an 'after', implying time. How often do you assume time will continue for you indefinitely? What can you do to live with the reality that your time in this world is limited?
?
The third proof claims that what is possible must exist. When you plan your life, do you rely more on what seems possible or on Allah's will? Examine one area where you've trusted possibility over divine decree—how can you shift your trust?

Ghazali's Refutation and the Role of Revelation
Ghazali's refutation of the third proof and final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the third argument for future eternity, his refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl, and his conclusion that future eternity is rationally possible but known only through revelation.

Core Idea
Reason allows the world to persist forever; revelation decides whether it will.

Ghazali now weakens the third proof and refutes an extreme view that denies infinite future cycles. He distinguishes between past and future eternity, arguing that reason does not forbid the world's endless preservation by God. The actual outcome, he insists, is known only from the Law (shar').

Guided Path
1
Ghazali's critique of the third proof
Ghazali argues that while past eternity is impossible (because it implies no beginning), future eternity is not impossible if God preserves the world.
He distinguishes between hadith (originated) and act: an act must have a beginning, but an originated thing does not necessarily have an end.
2
Refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl
Abu al-Hudhayl claimed infinite future cycles are as impossible as infinite past cycles. Ghazali rejects this because the future never fully enters existence.
The past has entered existence successively; the future is never actualized all at once.
3
Conclusion: Reason allows, revelation informs
Ghazali concludes that reason permits both preservation and annihilation. The actual state is known from the divine law, not rational speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
How does the rational possibility of the world's persistence impact your conviction about the Day of Judgment? Is your belief based on rational speculation or on certain knowledge from the Book?

intermediate

The First Two Arguments for the World's Perpetual Future
Ghazali states the issue and enumerates the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali presents the philosophers' first two arguments for the world's perpetual future (the causal argument and the cessation argument) and his assertion that the same objections from pre-eternity apply.

Core Idea
The philosophers' arguments for a perpetual future mirror those for pre-eternity.

This lesson introduces Ghazali's engagement with the philosophers' claim that the world is eternally perpetual. He begins by stating that this issue is a branch of the pre-eternity issue and that the same four proofs and objections apply. We then see the first two proofs: the causal argument (the effect must coexist with its eternal cause) and the argument from cessation (which implies time). This sets the stage for understanding Ghazali's critical approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the parallel structure with the pre-eternity discussion.
Pay attention to Ghazali's phrasing: 'والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق'
2
Identify the two proofs
Find where Ghazali introduces the first way (causal argument) and the second way (cessation argument). Understand the reasoning behind each.
The first proof hinges on the unchanging cause; the second on the implication of time from cessation.
3
Reflect on Ghazali's claim of identity
Note that Ghazali says the objections from pre-eternity apply identically here. Consider why this is significant for the overall refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [٢] فى إبطال قولهم فى أبدية العالم والزمان والمكان ليعلم أن هذه المسألة فرع الأولى، فإن العالم عندهم كما أنه أزلى لا بداية لوجوده، فهو أبدى لا نهاية لآخره، ولا يتصور فساده ولا فناؤه، بل لم يزل كذلك، ولا يزال أيضاً كذلك وأدلتهم الأربعة التي ذكرناها في الأزلية جارية في الأبدية، والاعتراض كالاعتراض من غير فرق. فإنهم يقولون؛ إن العالم معلول، وعلته أزلية أبدية فكان المعلول مع العلة، ويقولون: إذا لم تتغير العلة لم يتغير المعلول، وعليه بنوا منع الحدوث، وهو بعينه جار في الانقطاع، وهذا مسلكهم الأول ومسلكهم الثانى: أن العالم إذا عدم فيكون عدمه بعد وجوده، فيكون له ( بعد ) ففيه إثبات الزمان . ومسلكهم الثالث: أن إمكان الوجود لا ينقطع، فكذلك الوجود الممكن يجوز أن يكون على وفق الإمكان، إلا أن هذا الدليل لا يقوى، فإنا نحيل أن يكون أزليا، ولا نحيل أن يكون أبديا، لو أبقاه الله تعالى أبداً، إذ ليس من ضرورة الحادث أن يكون له آخر، ومن ضرورة الفعل أن يكون حادثاً، وأن يكون له أول. ولم يوجب أن يكون للعالم لا محالة آخر إلا أبو الهذيل العلاف، فإنه قال: كما يستحيل فى الماضى دورات لا نهاية لها، فكذلك في المستقبل؛ وهو فاسد، لأن كل المستقبل قط لا يدخل فى الوجود لا متلاحقاً ولا متساوقاً والماضى قد دخل كله فى الوجود متلاحقاً، وإن لم يكن متساوقاً، وإذ قد تبين أنا لا نحيل بقاء العالم أبداً من حيث العقل، بل نجوز إبقاءه وإفناءه، فإنما يعرف الواقع من قسمى الممكن بالشرع، فلا يتعلق النظر فيه بالعقول
Issue [2]: On refuting their claim regarding the perpetual future of the world, time, and space. Let it be known that this issue is a branch of the first. For the world, according to them, just as it is pre-eternal with no beginning to its existence, so it is perpetually future with no end to its final term. Its corruption and annihilation are inconceivable; rather, it has always been so, and will always be so. And their four proofs which we mentioned concerning pre-eternity apply to perpetual futurity, and the objection is the same objection without any difference. For they say: the world is an effect, and its cause is pre-eternal and perpetually future, so the effect accompanies the cause. And they say: if the cause does not change, the effect does not change; and upon this they based the denial of origination (al-huduth), and it is exactly the same concerning cessation. This is their first way. Their second way: that if the world were to cease to exist, its non-existence would be after its existence, so it would have an 'after', and in this there is an affirmation of time. Their third way: that the possibility (imkan) of existence is not cut off, so likewise possible existence may be in accordance with possibility. Except that this proof is not strong, for we deem it impossible that it be pre-eternal, but we do not deem it impossible that it be perpetually future, if God Most High were to preserve it forever. For it is not a necessary property of an originated thing that it have an end, and it is a necessary property of an act that it be originated and that it have a beginning. And no one made it necessary that the world necessarily have an end except Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, for he said: just as past cycles without end are impossible, so likewise in the future. And this is false, because all of the future never enters into existence, neither successively nor simultaneously, while the past has all entered into existence successively, though not simultaneously. And since it is clear that we do not deem the perpetual existence of the world impossible from the standpoint of reason, but rather we allow its preservation and its annihilation, then what actually occurs from the two divisions of the possible is known only by revelation (shar'), so rational consideration has no bearing on it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the issue: refuting the philosophers' claim that the world is perpetually future (abadi), just as they claim it is pre-eternal (azali). Ghazali notes that this issue is a branch of the first issue (the pre-eternity of the world). He then says that the four proofs used for pre-eternity also apply to perpetual futurity, and the objections are the same. He lists the first three proofs. The first proof: the world is an effect, and its cause (God) is eternal in both directions, so the effect must be co-eternal with the cause both in pre-eternity and in perpetual futurity. The second proof: if the world were to cease, its non-existence would be after its existence, implying a temporal 'after', thus affirming time. The third proof: the possibility of existence never ceases, so the existence of the possible may continue forever. However, Ghazali criticizes this third proof: we do not consider it impossible for the world to be perpetually future if God preserves it, but we do consider it impossible for it to be pre-eternal because an originated thing must have a beginning. He then refutes Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who argued that just as infinite past cycles are impossible, so are infinite future cycles. Ghazali calls this false because the past has actually entered existence successively, whereas the future never does, so an infinite past is impossible but an infinite future is not. Finally, Ghazali concludes that reason does not forbid the perpetual future existence of the world; both preservation and annihilation are rationally possible. Which one actually occurs is known only through revelation, not reason.

Key Terms
أبدية
أزلية
مسلك
علة
معلول
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The arguments for the world's pre-eternity do not apply to its perpetual futurity.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the four proofs for pre-eternity apply to perpetual futurity without any difference, and the objections are the same.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between the arguments for pre-eternity and those for perpetual futurity?
A) They are entirely different.
B) They are the same without any difference.
C) The arguments for perpetual futurity are stronger.
D) Only the causal argument applies to both.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' case for a perpetual future world is based on the same reasoning as for pre-eternity, and Ghazali's refutation will mirror that earlier discussion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that the world will never end, how would that change the way you live your life today? What would you prioritize differently?
?
The philosophers argued that because God is eternal, His creation must also be eternal. Have you ever unconsciously assumed that God's attributes force certain limitations on His actions? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink God's absolute freedom?
?
This passage shows that even the brightest minds can build entire systems on flawed assumptions about God. In your own understanding of Allah, what is one assumption you hold that might not be as solid as you think? Will you examine it today?
